745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 6 
 
 
1. Excellence
1.1 Objectives  
With the rapid growth and development of the human population, municipal solid waste (MSW) has contributed significantly to 
the total amount of waste generated by our society. Today in Europe, each person generates, in average, 475 kg of MSW every 
year1, this amount increasing at an annual rate of 10%. Around 40-50% of this amount correspond to bio-waste (food and kitchen 
waste fr om households, restaurants, caterers and retail premises, comparable waste from market areas and biodegradable 
garden and park waste),2 so that a total biomass of more than 100 million tons derived from MSW is yearly produced in the EU. 
There are two major streams: green waste/pruning residues (including 50 -60% water and lignocellulosic materials) and kitchen 
waste (no wood and up to 80% water). This organic fraction mainly contains carbohydrates (30 -40% cellulose, hemicellulose, 
starch, pectins), proteins  (5-15%) and lipids (10 -15%), which are all useful raw material that can be converted to biobased 
products. The valorisation of organic MSW not only helps to solve environmental pollution but also contributes to the transit ion 
from a linear to a renewable circular economy. 
Due to the increasingly restrictive MSW management legislation 3 in the last years there has been a strong shift from MSW 
landfilling to recycling of materials such as glass, plastics, paper or metals (11%) and energy production from the combustible 
fraction through incineration (26%). Digestion and composting (16%) have also contributed to the reduction of the biodegradable 
fraction of MSW sent to landfill1. It is estimated that the AD of the total organic fraction of municipal solid waste (OFMSW) produced 
in the world could generate enough biogas to contribute around 2% of the global energy consumption. However, despite this great 
AD potential to recover ene rgy, conventional AD only generates single low -value products, biogas and compost (Figure 1.1a). 
Almost 20 years later the Landfill Directive 1999/31/EC, and after the publication of the revised Waste Framework Directive 
2008/98/EC it is mandatory to impro ve the management of bio -waste in EU by supporting technical solutions that are able to 
generate higher value products. 
 
Figure 1.1a Current scenario for MSW treatment. 
 
This trend has been recently reinforced through the recent publication of the Circular Economy package COM(2015) 614 final of 
December 2, 2015 , Closing the loop an EU Action Plan for the Circular Economy (http://ec.europa.eu/environment/circular-
economy/index_en.htm) that includes the proposal for the modification of the Landfill, Waste Framework and Packaging Directives 
among others. A careful analysis of the baseline situation at EU -28 level about generation, collection, management and related 
policies ab out the MSW will be the starting point to evaluate how this biowaste flow could be diverted from actual local 
management systems into the proposed biorefinery/valorisation chain.  
The aim of URBIOFIN project is to demonstrate the techno -economic and environmental viability of the conversion of 
10 tonnes per day of the organic fraction of MSW (OFMSW) into: 
➢ Chemical building blocks (bioethanol, medium or short volatile fatty acids, biogas), 
➢ Biopolymers (low and medium chain polyhydroxyalcanoates, composites combining different PHA) 
➢ Additives (bioethylene, microalgae derived biochemicals). 
By using the biorefinery concept applied to MSW (urban biorefinery), URBIOFIN will exploit the OFMSW as feedstock, 
taking into account its heterogeneity and variable composition, to produce at semi -industrial scale different valuable 
marketable products (Table 1.1a). The processes involved in the development of these bioproducts will be 
interconnected so that a versatile and efficient biorefinery for OFMSW will be demonstrated (Figure 1.1b,c). URBIOFIN 
will offer a new feasible and more sustainable scenario alternative to the current treatment of the OFMSW. 
The project will validate the whole value chain (Figure 1.1b) at demonstration scale, taking into account the involvement of waste 
management authorities (URBASER and Municipalities) and the validation of the targeted products by end-users (LEYGATECH,

BPE and NATRUE). In addition, the project is clearly related with the b ioeconomy purposes of European Union (Figure 1.1b, in 
green 2nd picture) since the objective is going from a fossil -based economy to obtain certain products towards  a bioeconomy 
approach. At the same time the objectives of the project are aligned to RIS3 strategies from all European regions involved. 
   
Figure 1.1b Value-chain for MSW as renewable substrate and project position in bioeconomy  
Therefore the targeted products according to previous value-chain diagram will be in the URBIOFIN biorefinery will be: 
Table 1.1a Targeted biobased products to be obtained at demo scale during URBIOFIN project  
Biobased product Use/final application 
Bioethanol Chemical building block for bioethylene production and VFAs elongation

Dry Organic-Mineral Granules Solid Biofertiliser 
Therefore the evolution of the Figure 1.1a throughout this project will be to the following figure 1.1c . In contrast to the 
current MSW treatment, the URBIOFIN concept (see figure 1.1c) proposes a global approach that regards OFMSW as a promising 
new raw material for a higher added-value production. Thus, the URBIOFIN concept consists of an integrated biorefinery for 
the valorisation of OFMSW  to high -value products  treating at least 10 tonnes per day (during the project will be 
processed more than 3,000 tonnes), such polyhydroxyalcanoates (PHA) and bioethylene and chemical building blocks 
as bioethanol, volatile fatty acids and biogas. In this way the URBIOFIN concept  will include the following improvements to 
the baseline treatment plant: (1) the two -phase AD unit, aimed to obtain volatile fatty acids (VFAs) in the first stage and also 
biogas as chemical building block in the second stage  to produce PHA or biomethane (as final fuel for vehicles) ; (2) use of the 
bioethanol produced from the OFMSW for elongation of VFA to medium / long-chain fatty acids (MCFA/LCFA) that could represent 
a final product for cosmetic applications or a precursor for PHA production 3) Use of the bioethanol to produce Ethylene for fruit 
ripening  4) Use the VFA as carbon -source for PHA enrichment step 5) Use of the MCFA/LCFA as carbon-source for PHA 
accumulation as medium chain length PHA via pure culture. 6) Use of the VFA as carbon-source for PHA accumulation as short 
chain length polyhydroxyalcanoates and scl-PHA products via mixed culture 7) Use of the digestate from the second stage and 
the residual biomass from the later PHAs process to obtain solid fertilizers 8) Use of the microalgae biomass from the biogas 
upgrading process to produce aminoacids for high-value liquid fertilizers 9) Use of the biogas as carbon-source for PHA production 
in order to obtain bioplastics to be used for packaging/agriculture/household bags and 10) the mentioned mcl-PHA and scl-PHA’s 
in combination with various cellulose- and  lignin-containing pulp and other solid materials deriving from MSW will be applied to 
create a new biocomposite material.

745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 8 
 
 
 
Figure 1.1c Diagram of URBIOFIN project, showing the bio-based product lines 
In order to deliver on the above, the specific objectives that will be fulfilled over the course of URBIOFIN are discussed below: 
Table 1.1.b URBIOFIN objectives and performance indicators based on milestones 
Objective 1. To evaluate the heterogeneity and compositional variability of the OFMSW  for the design of the 
biorefinery and select representative feedstock (linked to WP1) 
The main challenge to use the organic fraction of MSW as feedstock for bio -based industries lies in its high complexity and 
variability in composition. The three main factors affecting th is variability are the type of waste collection system, the 
geographic location and seasonality. In URBIOFIN project, the biorefinery processes will be designed and adapted taking into 
account this non-homogeneous feedstock and its compositional variability, as well as the presence of inhibitors to downstream 
biotechnological processes in order to assure its industrial feasible conversion into bio-based products. Representative MSW 
samples will be studied and selected for the biorefinery demonstration activities. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 1  
Objective 2. To define the industrial requirements in terms of final bioproducts properties (Linked to WP1) 
It will be defined in detail the requisites (purity, technical properties of biopolymers, N and C content of biofertilisers, legislative 
requirements, etc.) that the bioproducts developed must fulfil to satisfy the requirements of final users in order to facilitate 
future commercialisation. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 2 
Objective 3. To reduce the operational costs of the bioethanol production from OFMSW by 20% (linked to WP2) 
Some of the bioethanol pilot plant modules (pre-treatment, simultaneous fermentation and sacharification modules, bioethanol 
separation units) will be adapted to the new feedstock conditions in order increase the bioethanol yield to be used as a 
chemical building block for bioethylene production. As one of its goals for the adaptation of these modules is to reduce 10% 
the energy costs of the plant . New monitoring technologies will improve the performance of the plant control by obtaining 
relevant parameter information through advanced sensors and adding an intelligent plant control platform. Novel customized 
enzymes cocktails for OFMSW developed by NOVO partner will improve the liberation of fermentable sugars from 65 to 80%, 
while minimising the presence of inhibitors to downstream processes.  
With the implementation of all these actions it is aimed to reduce the current state-of-the-art operational costs for bioethanol 
production from OFMSW by 20%.  
Performance indicator: Milestone 3 
Objective 4. To demonstrate at semi -industrial scale the viability for the continuous conversion of bioethanol 
produced from the OFMSW into bioethylene (linked to WP2) 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 9 
 
 
The continuous catalytic conversion of bioethanol from OFMSW into bioethylene will be demonstrated at semi -industrial-
scale. The process will be evaluated in technological terms to certif icate industrial feasibility. Target yield is up to 99% for 
bioethylene. Bioethylene produced will be in compliance with international standards and market regulations, and with 
comparable costs to comparable existing products to ensure future commercialisation. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 4 
Objective 5. To demonstrate at semi -industrial scale the continuous VFA production from partial AD of OFMSW 
(WP3) 
Demo-scale prototype for two phase AD system integrating hydrolytic and Methanogenic reactor will be developed in the 
project to treat OFMSW. At least 20g of VFA/L should be achieved in the first stage and degradation of 70% of soluble COD 
in the Methanogenic step is expected in the project. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 6 
Objective 6. To validate at semi-industrial scale at least 50-60% of operational yields for MCFA and PHA production 
at semi-industrial scale (WP3) 
Demo-scale prototype for production scl -PHA and mcl-PHA will be validated. On the  one hand directly from VFA from AD 
throughout two processes of enrichment of biomass and use this biomass to accumulate scl -PHA using always mix culture, 
and in the other hand throughout elongation of VFA to MCFA /LCFA with Cryptococcus or direct Ps eudomonas-mediated 
fermentation, to produce biomass and accumulation with pure culture. The expected yields will be around 40 -50% for scl-
PHA of biomass containing PHA and higher values (up to  
50-70%) for mcl-PHA. The final amount of both PHA types will be around 0.5 tonnes per year. 
 Performance indicator: Milestones 7 and 8  
Objective 7. To validate at semi-industrial scale PHA extraction process (WP3) 
PHA extraction based on combination of mechanical disruption with bead mills and enzymatic treatment will be applied to 
extract the PHA coming from scl-PHA (from VFA or biomethane) or for mcl-PHA. In all the cases the objective will be recover 
at least 60% of the accumulated PHA with good properties for further applications. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 9 
Objective 8. To validate at semi-industrial scale biogas upgrading using microalgae (WP4) 
Technological prototype for integral biogas upgrading, which combines the conversion of a MSW by-product into a consumer 
product (i.e. biogas into biomethane), the treatment of a waste effluent from anaerobic digestion (digestate is used as nutrient 
source and thus carbon and phosphorous contents are reduced by > 70%, with nitrogen reductions > 90%) and the production 
of a microalgae biomass (biomass productivities of 11 -13 g m -2d-1) for further revalorization as biofertiliser. The final 
biomethane will comply with current legislation (CH4>95%, CO2<2.5%, H2S<5 mg S m-3, O2<1%) to be used as a consumer 
product for natural gas grid injection or as vehicle fuel. 
Performance indicator: Milestone 10 
Objective 9. To produce new bio-based material coming from biogas (WP4) 
The novel two-stage process will be able to completely remove the H 2S and ~90-95% of the CH 4 contained in the biogas, 
producing ~0.7 g biomass per g of CH 4 treated. Under optimum operating conditions, the methanotrophic community will 
achieve a PHA accumulation capacity over 60% (w/w).  
Performance indicator: Milestone 11.  
Objective 10. To produce final marketable products from the bio-based chemicals, polymers and additives obtained 
in URBIOFIN biorefinery  and validate their performance (linked to WP5) 
In order to validate the whole value chain the bioproducts obtained in URBIOFIN biorefinery will be utilized by possible end 
users. Bioethylene will be used as ripening gas in post-harvest fruit chambers. PHA polymers will be used to produce plastic 
bags and agriculture plastic films, as well as cosmetic packaging. Dry granules fermented-digested solid fraction of OFMSW 
and aminoacids from microalgae will be tested as biofertiliser. Biomethane will be used directly as end -product or as PHA 
precursor depending on business plan (different countries have different pri ces for biomethane that can compensate or not 
going to other products). The performance of all these developed final products will be validated to demonstrate that they 
satisfy their expected functions. 
Performance indicator: Milestones 13, 14 and 15 
Objective 11. To demonstrate the technical, environmental and economic viability of the developed processes and 
products in URBIOFIN biorefinery as well as regulatory compliance (WP6) 
Life cycle assessment (LCA) and life cycle cost (LCC) along with techno -economic assessment (TEA) will be performed on 
the processes developed for the selected bioproducts in order to prove the reduction of environmental and economic impact 
vs. current traditional materials  and processes. Issues regarding health risks and regulatory assessment will be  studied, 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 10 
 
 
analysed and addressed during the projec t with the aim to ensure the compliance of the processes an d products from 
URBIOFIN project. T echno-economic viability of the b iorefinery with inputs from previous WPs will be evaluated  and 
optimised. 
Performance indicator: Milestones 16, 17 and 18 
Objective 12. To deliver the impacts of the URBIOFIN project for enabled innovation in the European biobased 
industries (WP7) 
Exploitation plans for sustaining the URBIOFIN concept beyond the project period will be created, whereby the vision is to be 
the key driver of the bioeconomy innovation in Europe. T his vision will be supported by a strong dissemination strategy to 
raise awareness about bioproducts derived from OFMSW among the bio-based industries and the general public. Business 
plans will be developed for the developed bioproducts to facilitate thei r market entry. Effective knowledge management 
activities will maximise IP potential. Engagement with Waste Management Authorities and Bio-based industry clusters will be 
promoted. 
Performance indicator: Milestones 19, 20 and 21.
1.2 Relation to the work programme   
The URBIOFIN project relates to the topic BBI 2016.D6: Valorisation of the organic content of Municipal Solid Waste and 
contributing to the renewable circular economy . In particular the project addresses one of the BBI 2016  priorities, which is 
the “Development of biorefinery technologies and efficient processes to increase the competitive position of the bio -based 
industries in Europe” (Scientific Priority 2). This includes the implementation of technologies to enable the processing of a variety 
of biomass feedstock, the optimisation of production routes for 2nd generation bio-based chemicals and intermediates in order to 
effectively replace 1 st generation sugars and the optimisation of bioprocesses, including bio -catalysis operations, by improving 
yields and cost competitiveness as compared to state-of-the-art. 
In order to effectively respond to the identified challenge we have mobilised a strong industry-academia partnership that integrates 
the required technologies and ski lls. Table 1.2a provides a summary of how URBIOFIN addresses the specific challenge and 
scope of BBI 2016.D6 topic.

1.3 Concept and approach 
1.3.1 Overall concept underpinning the project 
The biorefinery concept for OFMSW aims at maximizing the recovery of more diverse resources from MSW in addition to 
bioenergy, by simultaneous exploitation and remediation of waste. The urban biorefinery will be composed of three inter-related 
main modules where each value chain will be developed (Figure 1.3a). These modules will be demonstrated in three sites, in 
order to make use of already existing demo facilities. Additional investment will be done to these facilities to improve their efficiency 
and reach the targeted final bioproducts with the quality required for future market access. 
Module 1 will be located in Alcudia (Valencia, Spain), in the biotechnological demonstration plant of IMECAL project coordinator. 
IMECAL together with the partner CIEMAT have developed a biotechnological patented technology (European Patent EPO 
2112226, USA Patent 8399228 B2 and international PCT WIPO 099038) to produce second generation bioethanol from the 
OFMSW (PERSEO bioethanol ®). The plant has a capacity to process 25 tonnes per day of OFMSW. The necessary facilities to 
use the bioethanol produced as building block for the production of bioethylene will be constructed and integrated in IMECAL 
demonstration plant. 
Module 2 will be located in Zaragoza (Spain) and Puertollano (Ciudad Real, Spain), in the MSW Technology Innovation Center 
“Alfonso Maíllo” owned by URBASER partner and in the Biorefinery pilot plant of IRIAF. Specifically the 2 AD systems as well as 
the biogas line will be placed in Zaragoza and the downstream line for VFA valorisation to PHA will be placed in Puertollano. The 
final extraction step will be performed in Wageningen, since WR resources to perform this task, avoiding important investment 
costs to the process. 
Module 3 will be located in Zaragoza (Spain) to convert the biogas to biomethane with microalgae as well as validate its use as 
fuel for vehicles, and also its use for PHA production. Additionally in this module the biofertilisers uses will be evaluated against 
controls to verify the improved properties of these products.  
 
 
 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 12 
 
 
 
Figure 1.3a URBIOFIN modules 
Partners involved in URBIOFIN have large experience in the required technologies and materials developed in the project as well 
as both national and international recognition (Section 4). The different modules of the project have as objective to produce high 
value products from the or ganic fraction of mun icipal solid wastes, moving ahead  from the typical anaerobic  and composting 
processes. For this reason, the modules are thought to obtain high value products from 3 intermediates, bioethanol, VFA, MCFA 
and biogas.  
In the last decade bioethanol has become the largest biofuel with an estimated worldwide production of 150 billion litres in 20204. 
The rapid development of the bioethanol market has been accompanied by a growing interest in its use as a renewable building 
block for the production of bio-based chemicals5. In URBIOFIN project second generation bioethanol from OFMSW will be used 
as feedstock for the production of bioethylene, which is the raw material for the synthesis of PE (the most demanding plastic  in 
Europe, with more than 13 Mtones demanded in 2013)6. Bioethanol will be produced from OFMSW by using specially developed 
PERSEO technology, which will be adapted to convert the bioalcohol, currently employed as a liquid biofuel, to produce 
bioethylene at semi-industrial scale. The residual solid which contain the components of MSW that are not fermented to produce 
bioethanol (proteins, lipids, pectin , etc.) is nowadays being used as solid biofuel. However, in URBIOFIN this material will be 
employed as feedstock stream for downstream valorisation in the biorefinery, incorporating it to Anaerobic Digestion (AD) in order 
to valorise this fermentation feedstock into valuable bioproducts.  
The current commercially established process to biopolymers production is mainly based on fossil fuel pathway, with clear 
disadvantages regarding non-renewable source as well as environmental impact. In order to reach high PHA yields the typical 
bacterial feedstock are costly sugars, which in this project is avoided. The relatively high costs are the main reason which hinders 
biopolymers adoption in the market. The major advantage of this project lies at the reduction of costs using as substrate residual 
and renewable source non -food competing such as VFA from AD and MCFA /LCFA. It provides a very convenient option to 
decrease the production costs of biopolymers. Moreover, the biochemical pathways proposed in this project also offers a great 
advantage in terms of process flexibility, since you can use directly the VFA to produce scl -PHA for agriculture or low cost 
applications or you can apply the MCFA as substrate for accumulation PHA as mcl-PHA for high cost applications.  
The symbiotic activity between photosynthetic microorganisms (microalgae and/or cyanobacteria) and heterotrophic and 
chemoautotrophic bacterial communities in p hotobioreactors operated at high pH constitutes a low -cost and environmentally 
friendly technological platform for the simultaneous removal of CO 2, H 2S, NH 3 and VOCs from biogas . In this process, 
 
4 (OECD-FAO, 2014) http://ethanolproducer.com/articles/11264/oecd-fao-outlook-report-predicts-growth-in-ethanol-production 
5 Weusthuis, R.A., Aarts, J.M.M.J.G., Sanders, J.P.M., 2011. From biofuel to bioproduct: is bioethanol a suitable fermentation feedstock for synthesis of bulk 
chemicals? Biofuels, Bioproducts and Biorefining (5), 486–494. 
6 http://www.plastics.gl/market/plastics-the-facts-2014/  

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 13 
 
 
photosynthetic microorganisms employ the incident solar light to fix the CO 2 contained in the biogas as biomass, with the 
subsequent production of O 2. This O2 generated in-situ in the photobioreactor is used by chemoautotrophic bacteria to oxidize 
H2S and NH3 to SO42- and NO3-, respectively. The O 2 is also employed for the oxidation of VOCs to CO 2 and H2O as main end 
products. This oxygen demand for H2S, NH3 and VOCs oxidation avoids desorption of the photosynthetic oxygen present in the 
cultivation broth and therefore the contamination of the upgraded biomethan e with O 2. Further revalorization of the produced 
microalgae-bacterial biomass is possible via biofertiliser production. A final biomethane polishing step for volatile methyl siloxanes 
removal will be also implemented. The recently demonstrated ability of specific microbial communities for siloxanes 
biodegradation opens a new opportunity to biologically remove these contaminants commonly present in raw biogas in a more 
environmentally friendly and less energy-intensive way. Their low solubility requires an additional effort to overcome mass transfer 
limitations when implementing this process in conventional bioreactors, which will be addressed by innovative strategies for gas-
liquid transport intensification. Microalgae from the photo bioreactor used for biomethane upgrading can be used, once dried as 
component for solid biofertilisers formulations and/or used for extraction of its aminoacids (thanks to its high content in proteins) 
and other active molecules with agronomic properties for the formulation of l iquid biofertilisers. The production of PHAs will be 
addressed in a two-stage biological process consisting of a desulphurization step followed by a PHAs production unit. Autotrophic 
denitrification constitutes a cost-efficient biogas purification method based on the injection of NO 3- as electron acceptor for H 2S 
oxidation. This technology constitutes a potential alternative to aerobic biofiltration (as it avoids external oxygen supply and mass 
transfer limitations), and present clear advantages over their physical-chemical counterparts. Once pre -treated, the H 2S-free 
biogas will be supplemented to a high mass transfer bioreactor where type II methanotrophs able to accumulate PHAs will use 
the CH4 contained in the biogas as carbon source. The high CH4 content and the CH4/O2 ratio support biogas as an ideal substrate 
for methanotrophs growth and PHAs production. Different process and cultivating conditions will be tested in order to boost t he 
PHAs accumulation capacity.  
The trans -disciplinary areas considered  in this project are:  Chemical engineering, Biotechnology; Polymer chemistry; 
Microbiology; Mathematical modelling and sustainabil ity assessment methodologies; Green chemistry; Economics; Process 
control and monitoring and Plant engineering. 
1.3.2 Positioning of the project 
The technological developments of URBIOFIN have been positioned on the Technology Readiness Levels (TRLs) scale (Table 
1.3a). The starting point is for the most part TRL5 -6 (Technology Validated-demonstrated in industrially relevant environment). 
The starting TRL is supported by the previous experience of the partners in the processes involved and linked to a number of 
prior related projects. Technical and industrial knowledge will be exchanged among the project partners for obtai ning the 
successful integration of technologies in industrial or operational environment with the participation of end users, thereby reaching 
an overall TRL6-7 (Technology demonstrated in industrially relevant environment-system prototype demonstration in operational 
environment). For instance, the main studies related to partial AD to produce VFAs are from last 5 years7. They mainly worked at 
lab-scale and using different residues trying to optimize the process parameters to enhance acid production. Addi tionally, 
Kleerebezem et al.  8 (2015) indicated in a recent review that more work is needed to demonstrate the possibilities of PHA 
production after partial AD against traditional biogas systems for multiple substrates.  
Besides, preliminary results obtained by the UVa in the photosynthetic upgrading of biogas 9,10 together with previous studies 
described in literature shows the capacity of this system for the production of a biomethane complying with the required 
composition for natural gas grid injection or use as automotive fuel. The scale up potential of this process has been demonstrated 
at laboratory scale in a controlled environment (TRL 4). Within this activity a system prototype will be operated and optimiz ed in 
a real operation environment, demonstrating the viability of the system for final applications (TRL 7). The photosynthetic biogas 
upgrading is currently under evaluation in a 1.2 m2 open pond located outdoors at the facilities of UVA under the frame of national 
R&D project. The successful results obtained so far guarantee a successful scale up to the 273 m2 open pond targeted (TRL 7). 
The two-stage biodesulfurization-bioconversion system and the biological polishing unit for siloxane removal will be moved from 
TRL 3 and 4 to TRL 6 based on the previous know-how on industrial scale biotrickling filters for odour removal in sewage plants 
and bubble absorption columns for CO2 capture acquired by UVa in recent research contracts with the companies JSF hidráulica

(http://www.cubiertaflotante.com/en/areas/control-de-olores/) and Biogas Fuel Cell S.A. ( http://biogasfuelcell.com/). The 
microbiology of biogas desulfurization and PHA accumulation has been fully understood in previous national projects and the 
advance in TRL here proposed will be therefore focused on mass transfer issues during process scale -up, where the staff from 
the Department of Chemical Engineering and Environmental Technology of UVa has a proven expertise. In addition, UVa is 
currently conducting at URBASER facilities a scale-up of the anoxic biotrickling filter up to 200 L using real biogas under the frame 
of a research contract. 
Regarding MCL-PHA production resources, WR will use existing technology to produce mcl -PHA from the organic fraction of 
municipal solid waste. For this purpose, two parallel development lines will be used; 1. Use of existing mixed cultures, as present 
and described for wastewater treatment plants,  and feed these mixed cultures with medium  or long -chain-length fatty acids 
(MCFAs and LCFAs, respectively ), separately or as produced in the anaerobic digestion process; and 2. Replace the existing 
mixed culture with a (pure) culture of Pseudomonas putida and feed this with organic waste and MCFAs/LCFAs. To achieve this, 
at least 12 man months (MM’s) have been reserved to lift this part to TRL-level 5 and higher. 
WR will also use its biorefinery/pilot-plant set-up to improve extraction yields for regular and mcl-PHA from fermentation fluid. For 
this purpose, physical breakage (bead mill) of biomass will be used in combination with enzymatic treatment to reach maximal 
yield of PHA’s. With an estimated effort of 6 men-months (MM’s) also a TRL level of 5 and higher will be reached for large scale 
PHA-extraction from fermentation fluid/microbial biomass.

1.3.4 Overall approach and methodologhy 
Starting at TRL-4 or 5 as discussed in the previous Section 1.3.2. Positioning of the project, the overall approach and methodology 
of URBIOFIN project is aimed to an advancement from TRL-5 to TRL-7. To reach such an end, the set of the project’s activities 
are categorised as shown in Figure 1.3a, and as further described in the points following. 
Modules for demo-scale process and integration: 
- Module 1 includes a demonstration plant for the conversion of OFMSW into second generation bioethanol using PERSEO 
Bioethanol® technology patented by IMECAL and CIEMAT partners. This technological approach is based on an enzymatic 
process with a moderate pre -treatment developed for OFMSW as raw material, which avoids the presence of inhibitors to the 
fermentation process. PERSEO Bioethanol® demonstration plant in Alcudia is equipped with all the reactors, fermenters, and 
separation and distillation units needed for t ransforming the OFMSW to bioethanol. After that, the produced bioethanol will be 
converted to Ethylene by an ‘Ethanol to Ethylene’ (EtoE) demonstration plant. For the catalytic conversion of bioethanol into  
bioethylene a new module will be incorporated downstream in L’Alcudia demo plant. The bioethylene production unit will be a 
modular demonstration unit which contains all necessary equipment to achieve a high yield and proper quality of ethylene. The  
engineering study for the integration of these new modules into the existing bioethanol production facilities will be performed by 
CSIC and adapted to the demonstration capacities of PERSEO bioethanol plant. Finally the bioethylene produced will be validated 
as ripening agent in post harvest fruit cambers (WP5). 
- Module 2 will be design and construct as an integrated demo-scale plant for the valorisation of the organic fraction of municipal 
solid wastes (OFMSW) to high -value products, such as mcl -PHA and chemical buildin g blocks as biogas. The demonstration 
plant at the R&D Center of URBASER in Zaragoza will include two-phase AD unit of 100 and 40 m3 respectively, aimed to obtain 
volatile fatty acids (VFAs) in the first stage and also biogas as chemical building block for module 3. The bioethanol produced in 
module 1 will be also used for elongation of VFA to medium or long chain fatty acids (MCFA and LCFA respectively) and the use 
of the MCFA/LCFA as carbon-source for PHA accumulation as medium chain length polyhydroxyalcanoates (mcl-PHA products). 
A scale-up from lab to 100L fermenters will be performed using Cryptococcus and similar other yeasts in the process. As a result, 
effluent coming from t he hydrolytic reactor will be sent to Puertollano at IRIAF pilot plant where the demo plant for microbial 
elongation will be operated with real effluent by IRIAF partner under the supervision of WR in order to optimize the process and 
trying to reach a hig h conversion of VFA conversion to MCFA /LCFA. This MCFA can be used as final product for cosmetic 
application and also as substrate for mcl -PHA production in the next task. IRIAF, Ainia and WR will validate and operate the 
demo-scale plant for scl and mcl-PHA production that will be also placed in Puertollano to operate in batch mode. This will consist 
of two reactors (from 0.3 to 20 m3) In the particular case of scl-PHA production, both reactors will be required, so as one will be 
used for mixed-culture biomass production and the other one for scl-PHA accumulation. Both reactors in such case will operate 
with the VFA-rich effluent of the hydrolytic reactor of Task 3. 1, and biomass of the first reactor will be transferred to the second 
one to maximize scl -PHA accumulation. In the particular case of mcl -PHA production with pure culture (using Pseudomonas 
putida), one reactor will be used, using  the VFA-rich effluent and ethanol/LCFA to accumulate MCL-PHA. The reactor in which 
PHA production will take place, should be operated under stress conditions (temperature, oxygen and nutrients) to maximize the 
PHA accumulation.  Finally, a comprehensive data analysis will be performed in order to evaluate and define the best parameters 
for the whole value chain to scl -PHA and mcl-PHA production at demo-scale level. PHA coming from the latter process will be 
extracted from biomass by a combination of mechanical treatment (Bead Mill) and enzymatic treatment (protease, lysozyme, 
SDS/EDTA). This will be done in WR installations in collaboration with IRIAF and NATURE PLAST. The use of the extracted mcl-
PHA for cosmetic bioplastics production and MCFA as cosmetic component will be evaluated by NATRUE as end user. The use 
of the extracted scl-PHA will be evaluated by LEYGATECH as end user for agricultural bioplastics. 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 17 
 
 
- Module 3 will comprise a combination of different strategies for the revalorization of the raw biogas obtained as a by -product 
from MSW anaerobic digestion. First, the operation of a symbiotic microalgae -bacteria process implemented in a 273 m2 high 
rate algal pond photobioreactor interconnected to an external absorption unit that will be constructed and operated at the R& D 
Center of URBASER in Zaragoza as a platform technology for integral upgrading of biogas in a single process to produce a high-
quality biomethane. In addition to this, a novel biological technology for the removal of volatile methyl siloxanes will be developed 
to be implemented as a refining stage in integral biogas upgrading systems. In one hand, the concentrated algal biomass will be 
hydrolyzed to convert protein into free amino acids. The performance of chemical and enzymatic hydrolysis will be tested and 
optimized in a 10-20 L reactor to obtain an optimal conversion efficiency of protein to amino acids. On the other hand, the digestate 
from the second stage and the residual biomass from the later PHA process of module 2 will be valorised using a Spouted Bed 
Dryer Technology (SPBD technology) to obtain dry granules as solid fertilizer. Produced aminoacids as a compound for liq uid 
fertilizers and the solid f ertilizer will be evaluated by BPE  as end user. Another valorisation approach will be based on the 
intensification of PHAs production from the CH4 contained in the biogas. After an initial biogas pretreatment for H 2S removal via 
anoxic biofiltration, the cultivation conditions of PHA-accumulating methanotrophs and the CH4 mass transport from the biogas to 
the liquid phase will be optimized to boost the production of the target bio -products. Two 10 m3 bioreactors will be operated for 
the anoxic removal of H 2S from biogas and the subsequent bioconversion of CH 4 into added value biopolymers (PHAs) using a 
high mass transfer unit.  
The three modules described above will be integrated in one whole process which includes the three product lines. The integration 
process in WP5 is aimed to prove the scaling -up and the technical (and later econom ic) feasibility of the URBIOFIN concept in 
order to progress for a future commercialisation of the process. The outcomes will be used for the en vironmental and economic 
assessment of the project. 
1.3.5 Sex and gender analysis 
The technologies, processes and materials developed in URBIOFIN are gender -independent. All URBIOFIN Partners have 
internal gender equality jobs creation and promotion policies. The consortium will foster the presence of women in the technical 
and management teams and decision board implemented to govern URBIOFIN project. The coordinator of URBIOFIN is Ms. Coll. 
Project aims to contribute to these objectives for enhancing Gender Equ ality by involving numerous female researchers (see 
section 4) in the project and by promoting women researchers to play a leading role in work packages . Actually, WP1  (Ms. 
Leticia Regueiro), WP2 (Ms. Caterina Coll), WP3 (Ms. Cristina Álvarez), WP5 (Ms. Natalia Herrero), WP6 (Ms. Sara Tena) 
and WP8 (Ms. Caterina Coll) are managed by female researchers, and as it is described in section 4, near to 50% of the staff 
researchers involved in the project are women. 
The Treaty on European Union, the European policy establishes equal opportunities between women and men. In par ticular 
articles 2 and 3 establish equality between women and men as a specific task of the Community and also to promote equality. 
Monitoring the progress toward gender equality in science has become a well-established activity of the European Union research 
policy. In accordance with these principles, the approach of URBIOFIN is fully in line with Directive 2002/73/EC of the European 
Parliament and of the Council of 23 September 2002 on the implementation of the principle of equal treatment for men and women 
as regards access to employment, vocational training and promotion, and working conditions.
1.4 Ambition 
1.4.1 Advance of the project beyond the state-of-the art 
Below are described the ambitious advances brought by URBIOFIN development starting with a short summary of current state-
of-the-art and the proposed developments in the project. 
State of the art in the bioethanol production from the OFMSW 
The conventional fermentation process is a commercial success in the conversion of edible biomass (mainly sugar in Brazil and 
starch in the USA) to first generation bioethanol. However, due to the competition with food and concerns about ecological 
systems, recent research has shifted towards bioethanol production from non-food biomass feedstocks (lignocellullosic materials 
from agricultural, industrial and urban wastes), which are low cost and abundantly available sources. The growing market for 
bioethanol as a biofuel has also been accompanied by an increasingly interest as a useful platform molecule for the productio n 
of value-added chemicals (some examples are: ethylene, propylene, 1,3-butadiene, acetaldehyde, diethyl ether, ethylene oxide, 
n-butanol, acetic acid and ethyl acetate)16.  
 
16 Weusthuis, R.A., Aarts, J.M.M.J.G., Sanders, J.P.M., 2011. From biofuel to bioproduct: is bioethanol a suitable fermentation feedstock for synthesis of 
bulk chemicals? Biofuels, Bioproducts and Biorefining (5), 486–494 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 18 
 
 
Second generation bioethanol from OFMSW can be produced by thermochemical and biochemical methods17. In thermal process, 
bioethanol is produced via gasification of the feedstock at a high pressure and further catalytic conversion of the syngas in to 
bioethanol. In the biochemical pathway, the substrate is firstly pre -treated in order to make the cellulosic component accessible 
to the cellulases for the subsequent enzymatic saccharification step. Sugars -mainly glucose- are released after the enzymatic 
hydrolysis and are then fermented to produce bioethanol. The processes usually employed in the fermentation of hydrolysate are 
simultaneous saccharification and fermentation (SSF) and separate hydrolysis and fermentation (SHF). Conventionally, the SHF 
process has been employed but SSF is superior for ethanol production since it can improve et hanol yields by removing end 
product inhibition and eliminate the need for separate reactors, which is also cost effective18. 
Today, there are several technologies for the conversion of OFMSW into second generation bioethanol, all of them at 
demonstration scale. Some of these processes have been developed by Bluefire, Coskata, New Planet Energy or Fulcrum. 19 In 
these projects not only OFMSW is used as feedstock, but also agroindustrial wastes, where it is pre-treated by severe and costly 
thermochemical processes. PERSEO Bioethanol®, patented by IMECAL and CIEMAT partners (European Patent EPO 2112226, 
USA Patent 8399228 B2 and international PCT WIPO 099038) is developed and specialized exclusively to the bioconversion of 
OFMSW, and it is based on an enzymatic process with a moderate pre-treatment, which avoids the presence of inhibitors to the 
fermentation process. PERSEO Bioethanol® demonstration plant is equipped with all the reactors, ferm enters, and separation 
and distillation units needed for transforming the OFMSW to bioethanol. The typical ethanol yields obtained vary from 100 – 130 
L of bioethanol per tonne of organic waste in dry bases. The bioethanol production involves several steps:  
A. The raw material is subjected to a pre -treatment which aims to eliminate the inerts contained in the organic matter (stones, 
plastics, metals, glass, etc.).  
B. A thermochemical dilute acid hydrolysis of the raw material is done in order to produce a solubilizing of the cellulose and 
hemicellulose contained in the MSW.  
C. After the feedstock is hydrolysed, a SSF process is done. The cellulose saccharification and fermentation of glucose to ethanol 
is produced in the same fermenter, adding the enzymes, yeast and nutrients suitable for efficient conversion of sugars to ethanol.  
D. The fermentation sludge obtained is subjected to a distillation process in order to concentrate the ethanol produced in the 
fermentation of the OFMSW.  
E. Dehydration process is carried out of the mixture by passing through a molecular sieve to achieve dehydration required for 
ethanol (> 99.5%) as a final product.  
The main challenges of the biochemical route concern the integration of the system which requires a coordinated effort in 
improving each step to achieve an overall high efficiency and a decreased production cost of bioethanol. The improvement of 
OFMSW pretreatment processes and the introduction of the latest biotechnological advances to decrease the operational costs 
(specifically those focused on the development on new generation of tailor made enzyme cocktails, which are able to effectively 
depolymerize cellulose and starch) have allowed to reduce considerably the price of MSW bioethanol produced. 
 
Figure 1.4a Pictures from the PERSEO Bioethanol® Technology plant 
Advances brought by URBIOFIN Few technologies for effectively converting municipal solid waste residues to fermentable sugars 
have been developed, from technical and economical point of view. URBIOFIN will use PERSEO Bioethanol® technology to 
produce bioethanol from MSW to be used as chemical building block, in order to improve the operation process costs through the 
use of most cost -effective pretreatments, and tailor made enzyme cocktails supplied by NOVOZYMES partner for the specific 
process. New generation of enzyme cocktails will be used in URBIOFIN demonstration activities. These new enzymes cocktails, 
targeting for OFMSW, increase the amount of liberated fermentable sugars and, as a consequence, the final bioethanol yield, 
which improves the process economy. Moreover the bioetha nol produced from the OFMSW will be employed as a chemical 
building block for the production of bioethylene, which is a higher added value application than the current use as biofuel, adapting 
 
17 Progress in bioethanol processing. Balat M, Balat H and Oz C. Progress in Energy and Combustion Science, 2008, 34, 551-573. 
18 Bioethanol production from agricultural wastes: An overview. N Sarkar, SK Ghosh, S Bannerjee, K Aikat. Renewable Energy, 2012, 37,19-27. 
19 www.bluefireethanol.com, www.coskata.com, www.newplanetenergy.com, www.fulcrum-bioenergy.com  

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 19 
 
 
the process to this new application. The minimum purity of the bioethanol required to produce bioethylene (95%) is lower than 
when it is used as biofuel (99.5%), which will reduce considerably the operational costs of the plant (molecular sieve drying  unit 
avoided). 
State of the art in the use of bioethanol as building block for the production of bioethylene  
The rapid development of the bioethanol market in the last decade has been accompanied by a growing interest in its use as 
renewable feedstock for the manufacture of bio -based chemicals20. Moreover, bioethanol was recently identified as one of the 
potential top bio-based raw materials for the chemical industry21. Important technological advances have also been made in the 
catalytic conversion of bioethanol (e.g. dehydrogenation, reforming, dehydratation, oxidation, etc.) and many significant chemicals 
have been successfully synthesized: ethylene, propylene, diethyl ether, 1,3-butadiene, acetaldehyde, ethyl acetate, etc22. Given 
the large number of options for bioethanol conversion Posada and coworkers 23 identified the most promising derivatives from a 
sustainability point of view (covering economic, environmental and health and safety aspects), being ethylene one of the most 
favourable compounds. The authors identified the most promising bioethanol derivatives by com paring the bioethanol -based 
conversion routes to its respective petrochemical counterparts.  
Nowadays, most of the global production of ethylene comes from the petrochemical industry by steam cracking of naphta. 
However, the catalytic conversion of bioetha nol to ethylene is a mature technology with molar yields reaching above 99% 24.    
There are many industrial examples, most of them in Asia with capacities varying from 40 KTA to 300 KTA. Most of these plants 
producing bioethylene use first generation bioethanol as feedstock (corn, starch, sugarcane). Since 2010 the Brazilian company 
Braskem commercializes “I'm greenTM Polyethylene”, a bioPE produced from ethanol sugarcane, having an annual production 
capacity of 200,000 tonnes25. In 2016, Technip have announced the acquisition of Hummingbird® technology from BP Chemicals 
Limited, a technology, which uses a wide range of ethanol feedstocks and can easily be integrated into existing ethylene facilities. 
The advanced catalyst simplifies the processing scheme and significantly increases selectivity. 
(http://www.technip.com/en/press/technip-completes-acquisition-hummingbird%C2%AE-technology-bp-chemicals-limited) 
Beyond the state of the art in URBIOFIN project bioethylene will be produced at demonstration scale from the catalytic conversion 
of second generation bioethanol coming from OFMSW. This bioethylene w ill be used as a ripening gas in post -harvest fruit 
chambers. Moreover, it could also be polymerized to produce a more sustainable and economically competitive bio-polyethylene 
compared to commercial bio-PE from sugarcane.  
State of the art in the production of VFA from anaerobic digestion of OFMSW 
The objective of the partial anaerobic digestion (AD), i.e two-phase AD, consist into induce the VFA accumulation, separating the 
hydrolytic phase from the methanogenic step which has been validated for multiple wastes, such as municipal solid waste26. To 
promote this biological VFAs production, maximizing the product concentration, but also controlling the product spectrum (type of 
individual VFAs produced) several parameters should be taken into account (i.e. hydraulic retention time, pH, temperature, etc.). 
The main drawback of this technology, even optimized to produce high VFAs concentrations, is that the VFAs mixture has no 
clear economical value. The specific VFAs have to be purified from the liquid effluent to be commercialized as pure acids. However 
the purification methods, such as precipitation, distillation or electrodialysis imply high cost in the process.

To surpass this economic issue in the URBIOFIN project the effluent from partial AD will be used directly as nutrient and carbon 
source for biological processes requiring minimum purification steps for medium -chain fatty acids (MCFA) production. The 
economical potential of novel routes producing higher added value products will enhance the development of this type of systems 
for several organic substrates.  Additionally these partial AD process produces a solid stream that contains different levels of non-
VFA organic matter as well as that can be perfectly applied to further biogas production in the methanogenic step.  
The advances proposed in URBIOFIN beyond the state of the art are focused in the use of VFA as chemical building block 
avoiding expensive process for its purification, looking at least for concentrations around 20 g/L in the hydrolytic efflue nt to be 
further processed.  
 
State of the art in the production of PHA biopolymer from VFA 
Medium- and long- chain fatty acids are more valuable than short -chain fatty acids (SCFA, e.g., acetic, propionic, butyric acid) 
from an economical point of view. SCFAs are major products in the first stage of anaerobic fermentation, and only feeding ethanol 
to this effluent, MCFA /LCFA formation is enhanced through chain elongation. Anaerobic microorganisms, such as Clostridium 
kluyveri which are already present in the anaerobic fermentors leading to VFA are known to elongate short-chain acids to primarily 
C6 and C8 by combining them with ethanol. In order to maximize MCFA/LCFA production, major parameters affecting the process 
are ethanol concentration and temperature. For production of even longer MCFA’s, such as C10 – C18 a separate fermentation 
using dedicated microorganisms such as Cryptococcus is proposed. By applying specific fermentation conditions and specific 
variants of this yeast, different product ranges for MCFA’s can be achieved such as C8-C12 or C14-C18. Another advantages of 
the elongation process in this project is related to the use of residual bio-ethanol coming from OFMSW undergoing a hydrolysis 
and fermentation step combined, known as simultaneous sacharification and fermentation (SSF), previously explai ned. This 
residual stream might be directly used for chain elongation, avoiding additional cost due to reagents purchase. The main 
disadvantage of the elongation process is the lower MCFA/LCFA production rates. For this reason in this project the objective will 
be used a reactor configuration with biomass retention to favour the enrichment of MCFA/LCFA producers and avoids the longer 
start-up, despite the additional cost of the carrier material27. The different MCFAs will also be used as a substrate to 
polyhydroxyalcanoates (PHA) producing microorganisms. Normally the short VFA are used as direct feeding for PHA 
accumulating bacteria, however in this the mixed PHA -producing culture and a pure culture of Pseudomonas putida will be fed 
with the MCFA’s/LCFAs generated in the elongation process. However medium-chain PHAs (mcl-PHA) appear to be much more 
elastomeric and resistant, and they possess attractive properties such as low melting points, high elasticity, and biodegradability, 
respect to scl-PHA, offering higher value applications28,29.

The advances proposed in URBIOFIN beyond the state of the art are the use of residual stream for the first AD reactor to produce 
high added value products, such as scl-PHA, mcl-PHA, with good properties respect to typical PHA produced with sugar or other 
expensive substrates. The objective will be produced around 100 g per day, increasing the percentage accumulated from typical 
30% at least till 50-70%. 
 
State of the art in biogas upgrading by microalgae photobioreactor 
Biogas is the byproduct from anaerobi c digestion with highest valorization potential, with an expected production in Europe in 
2030 of 20 billion Nm 3/year according to the Biogas European Association. Due to its high CH 4 content, biogas constitutes a 
suitable raw material for the production, via upgrading, of a biofuel similar to natural gas: biomethane. Nonetheless, biogas 
relatively high production cost, the lack of tax incentives and the inherent presence of pollutants such as CO 2, H2S, NH3, VOCs 
and volatile methyl siloxanes, have limited the use of biomethane for injection in natural gas grids or as automotive fuel. In this 
sense, the high production, upgrading, compression and distribution costs results in biomethane prices ~0.6 € Nm-3, considerably 
higher than those of natural gas (~0.4 € Nm-3) 30,31. It is therefore clear that a reduction in the cost of biogas upgrading based on 
technological advances will increase the competitiveness of this bioresource. Current scenario for biogas upgrading is mainly  
based on stand-alone physical-chemical technologies capable of removing either CO 2 or H2S. CO2 abatement is performed by 
highly energy-intensive technologies (0.25-0.3 kWh Nm-3) which entail high inversion costs (1,500 -6,000 € (Nm 3/h)-1), such as 
chemical scrubbers, pressure s wing adsorption or cryogenic separation 32. Similarly, physical -chemical technologies for H 2S 
removal involve prohibitively high operating costs and an excessive carbon footprint33. New developing biotechnologies for biogas 
upgrading, although more cost -efficient and environmentally friendly than their physical -chemical counterparts, still present 
important drawbacks which limit their full -scale implementation. For instance, CO 2 archaeal bioconversion by H 2 injection in 
digesters presents limited applicabili ty while posing explosion risks, and H 2S biological oxidation by air or O 2 injection entails 
operational problems related with elemental sulphur accumulation or biomethane contamination  with O 234. Thus, despite the 
promising resource recovery potential of biogas, there is no available technology in the market (either physical -chemical or 
biological) able to simultaneously remove CO2, H2S, NH3, VOCs and siloxanes from biogas in an efficient, economically feasible 
and environmentally sustainable way, and capable of producing in a single stage a biomethane with enough quality for its injection 
in the natural gas grid or its use as automotive fuel 35. One of the proposed technologies for biogas revalorization relies on the 
symbiotic activity of algae and bacteria  in photobioreactors, which can support the simultaneous removal of CO 2 and H2S in a 
single process36. Other biogas contaminants, such as NH 3 or VOCs, will be also likely oxidized in the process. This innovative 
process has been successfully implemented in UVA laboratory in open photobioreactors interconnected to an external absorption 
column to promote CO2 and H2S gas-liquid mass transfer from the raw biogas to the cultivation media (Figure 1.4d). Preliminary

results achieved CO2 and H2S removals over 95% and 100% respectively, and further optimization of the operating parameters 
resulted in a biomethane composition complying with most European legislations37,38. This system thus presents a high potential 
for scale-up, offering a promising upgrading capacity together with low energy consumption and environmental impacts.  
The presence of volatile methyl siloxanes  (VMS) has also consistently limited the energy use of biogas due to their detrimental 
effect on the equipment and piping of the process. Current physical-chemical processes for VMS abatement (such as adsorption, 
absorption or condensation) entail prohibitiv e investment and operating costs 39. The development of low -cost biological 
alternatives able to efficiently remove VMS by combining high -mass transfer units together with highly efficient microorganisms 
will be also addressed in this project. A novel final  polishing stage to be implemented after the upgrading process will be thus 
obtained. Revalorization of biogas via bioconversion into PHAs (biocompatible and biodegradable biopolymers) constitutes 
another attractive alternative due to their high market value (1.5 – 5 € kg-1PHA). In this sense, H2S-free biogas obtained via anoxic 
biofiltration, is an ideal substrate for cultivating type II methanotrophs able to accumulate PHAs as a result of its high CH4 content, 
the high ratio CH4/O2 and the low cost associated to this raw material. Previous literature studies and the experience of the UVA 
research group on PHAs production from diluted CH4 emissions support the potential of this valorization strategy40. This process 
will be implemented in high mass transfer bioreactors, able to remove the H2S present in the biogas by NO3- injection and further 
convert the CH4 into PHAs. 
 
 
Figure 1.4d Photobioreactor configuration for integral biogas upgrading 
Beyond the state of the art, URBIOFIN will validate two technological platforms for raw biogas revalorization at demonstratio n 
scale. Biomethane will be produced at a sufficient quality to be use d as vehicle fuel by photosynthetic upgrading of the waste 
biogas in a prototype photobioreactor. Siloxanes contained in the biogas will be removed in a final polishing step to ensure a high 
grade, competitive biomethane. The biogas will be also used as raw material to produce PHAs, a high added value product, in a 
two-stage bioreactor prototype.    
State of the art in the production of bio-fertilisers from microalgae and OFMSW 
Biofertilisers and Biostimulants  could be defined as “Probiotics” for plants: Products bringing out benefits from interactions 
between microorganisms – plants – environment. They are newly produced and are n ot regulated in all the EU member states . 
The new (revised ) Fertiliser regulation that is in course foresees an EU harmonised frame for Biofertilisers and Biostimulants . 
“Roadmap for the Revision of the Fertilisers Regulation (EC) No 2003/2003” indicates that “..., both producing industries and 
Member States are interested in extending  the scope of Regulation (EC) 2003/2003 to also cover organic fertilisers, organo -
mineral fertilisers, soil improvers, growing media, agronomic fertiliser additives and possibly plant biostimulants to achiev e full 
harmonisation of the legislation for the entire sector....”.  
Regarding the Regulatory frame for biofertilisers in Europe  the situation is confusing an there is no reference in the available 
revised Fertiliser Regulation draft of the inclusion of products with living microorganisms under the chapt er of Biostimulants. Lot

The important aspects to take into account for fertiliser are:

Different scientific works and field trials previously performed by the members of the consortium have indicated the interest  of 
combining rich C substrates like compost (biologically rich) or biochar (biologically inert material) together and with selected 
microorganisms strains or its cocktails fo r great biostimulant, fortifier and/or plant protection effects. One problem to be solved 
when combining biofertilisers or microorganisms with compost, soil improvers and growing media (and digestate and biochar) is 
the competition and interactions of selected strains and the natural populations in the organic substrate. Other problems could be 
the substrate possible toxicity and adequacy of the nutrient profile, and also excess temperatures in the substrate (over 35ºC ) 
can cause survival or functionality problems. 
The advances proposed in URBIOFIN beyond the state of the art are to produce specif ic solid biofertiliser combining the solid 
fraction from the second anaerobic digestion with specific co-substrates as well as liquid biofertiliser with high aminoacids content 
coming from microalgae hydrolysis to be applied in mixtures with several microorganisms or additives to make competing products 
in the market with high added value that will be validated against controls to verify good properties.

2.1.2 Improvement of innovation capacity and integration of new knowledge 
URBIOFIN will demonstrate at semi-industrial scale the OFMSW valorisation for the production of higher value and marketable 
products rather than conventional MSW processing (incineration for energy recovery o composting). The valorisation of OFMSW 
as a sustainable raw material creates an important competitiveness opportunity for the European bioeconomy and will increase 
the innovation capacity of our industry. It is expected that the industrial biotechnology market sector in Europe almost double by 
2050, compared to 2013 values, reaching up to 50 billion € 41. Bioethanol, bio based plastics and aminoacids (all of them target 
bioproducts obtained in U RBIOFIN proposed biorefinery) are expected to be the top three products with higher growth (Figure 
2.1a). In EU28 2013, the chemicals and plastics sector represented a turnover of 48 billion € and 160,000 jobs42. Initiatives such 
as URBIOFIN will enable in dustrial innovation in many different sectors to convert OFMSW as a raw material to produce bio 
based products with countless final applications. Our approach will significantly contribute to the bio -based industries ambitious 
target aiming to replace 30 % of the oil based chemical and materials in Europe by 203043, while at the same time helping to solve 
a great societal and environmental problem which is the MSW management.  
 
Figure 2.1a Industrial biotechnology market in Europe44 
The strong industrial composition of URBIOFIN consortium (7 SMEs and 2 large companies) together with research institutions 
(2 universities, 3 RTOs) and 2 clusters will enhance the innovative potential of the project. Industrial partners are integrated into 
the project decision making structure to ensure that the involved industrial sectors drive project activities and benefit from the 
project results. URBIOFIN will validate the whole value chain with the direct participation of waste management authorities a nd 
end-users of the developed bioproducts , which will guarantee high impact and effective dissemination and exploitation of the 
results. High economic impact will be achieved through strengthening the competitiveness of the industrial partners in the target 
market sectors and related ones by: 1) promoting the use of URBIOFIN products, components and systems in high value-added 
applications while keeping costs competitive, 2) strengthening and differentiating the EU Industry, 3) increasing workers’ sk ills. 
Detailed information of the market for each developed bioproducts in URBIOFIN (bioethanol, MCFAs, biomethane, as building 
blocks and bioethylene, PHA, biocomposites, solid and liquid biofertilisers as final products) is presented in the following pages. 
Bioethanol: Main current use of bioethanol is as transport biofuel or fuel additive for gasoline. Bioethanol has also been used in 
the beverage industry and as renewable chemical building block for the production of ethylene, propylene, diethyl ether, 1,3 -
butadiene, acetaldehyde, ethyl acetate, etc. 45 (Figure 2.1b). Global bioethanol market volume was 100 billion litres in 2014 46. 
However, it is expected a bioethanol production of 150 billion litres in 2020 47, which means an increase of 50% compared to 
current market volumes. Currently bioethanol is produced from edible biomass, mainly from sugar in Brazil and starch in USA 
(Figure 2.1c). However, due to the competition with food and concerns about ecological systems, recent research has shifted 
towards bioethanol production from non-food biomass feedstocks (lignocellullosic materials from agricultural, industrial and urban 
wastes), which are low cost and abundantly available sources. Second generation lignocellulosic ethanol is near 
commercialisation, with first-of-a-kind commercial plants built or currently under construction.

Figure 2.1b Worldwide production and use of ethanol48 
 
Figure 2.1c Production of bioethanol per country or region in 201349 
MCVFAs Medium-chain fatty acids with carbon chain lengths varying from 8 to 14 have a broad range of applications as lubricants, 
creams, soaps, antimicrobials, and edible oils. They are not constituents of fossil fuels and relative complicated chemical 
processes are needed to make them from longer-chain fatty acids. 
Caprylic acid is widely used in the cosmetic, food and feed industry for its antimicrobial activity. Decanoic acid is applied  in the 
chemical industry for its lubrication properties, but also in the cosmetic industry as lubricant but also becau se of its flavour 
properties. Lauric acid, as sodium salt, is soap and as such is applied in most detergents, soaps and other cleansing agents used 
in the Home & Personal Care and Cosmetic industry. It is also more-and-more applied as antimicrobial ingredient in Food & Feed 
Industry. Myristic acid is mostly applied in the Cosmetic Industry. The total fatty acid market size is expected to hit 12,000 M€ in 
2017, about 40% of this market in the personal care (detergent, soap, cosmetics) sector. The current market size for caprylic acid 
is approximately 2.5 M€. The market size for decanoic acid is approximately 50 M€, for lauric acid approximately 2 ,000 M€ and 
for myristic acid approximately 600 M€. The fatty acid market size is expected to double in the next 5-6 years in both the cosmetic 
and food applications. 
Biomethane: Biogas obtained as a by-product from the anaerobic treatment of OMSW represents a renewable energy source 
based on its high CH 4 content. The actual European network of ~14 ,000 anaerobic digesters is expected to increase within the 
next years in order to supply up to 18-20 million Nm3 in 2030 according to the latest European Biogas Association’s estimations. 
In this context, the development of new biotechnologies for biogas revalorization via its upgrading to biomethane will contri bute 
to the EU objective of developing a new European bio-economy less dependent on fossil fuels and based on the use of biological 
raw materials and bio-processing methods. For instance, one of the main direct uses of biomethane is as automotive fuel, which 
can be applied with a similar compositional standard as pipeline natural gas. A very rough estimation of current biomethane use 
worldwide for automotive purposes is 2 -3 TWh/a, which is expected to rapidly increase up to 6 TWh/a according to recent 
evaluations. However, and despite the interest and potential competitiveness of biomethane for fuel production, important limiting 
factors such as low economic efficiency or the low acceptance of the actual feedstock still challenge biomethane future prospects. 
URBIOFIN will develop a novel alternative to increase the competitiveness of biomethane against other fossil fuels as natural gas 
or diesel. First of all, the use of biogas from the digestion of OMSW ensures the sustainability of the feedstock used for biomethane 
production. Moreover, the upgrading system based on a biological pho tosynthetic process will significantly reduce the actual 
market price of biomethane. In this sense, biomethane actual cost amounts to ~0.6 € Nm-3, significantly higher than that of natural 
gas (about 0.4 € Nm-3), being the upgrading process one of the main contributors (~25-30%) to the total cost. Finally, the use of 
 
48 Angelici, C.; Weckhuysen, B. M.; Bruijnincx, P. C. A.; ChemSusChem 2013, 6, 1595. 
49 http://ethanolrfa.org/pages/World-Fuel-Ethanol-Production

biomethane to replace natural gas can lead to a significant reduction of greenhouse gases (GHG) emissions, achieving savings 
of over 80% when compared to fossil fuel alternatives. This will also contribute to the ambitious international commitments of the 
EU regarding reduction in GHG emissions together with the new goals of producing a clean energy. In summary,  the 
supplementation of a suitable feedstock for biogas production and the upgrading and conditioning to biomethane are key hotspots 
that will be addressed by URBIOFIN to ensure the economic and environmental sustainability of the final biomethane.  
Bioethylene: Ethylene is primarily a monomer used as a feedstock in the manufacture of polymer plastics (PE, PET, PEG, PVC 
and PS), fibers and other organic chemicals that are ultimately consumed in the packaging, transportation and construction 
industries and in a multitude of industrial and consumer markets. Total worldwide production of ethylene was 127 million tons in 
2014, being one of the most consumed chemicals50. Polyethylene (PE), the largest ethylene derivative market, accounted for over 
60% of global ethylene consumption in 201451. During 2009–2014, world ethylene consumption grew at an average rate of almost 
4.5% per year, while capacity increased at a slower rate of about 3.5%. Ethylene consumption is expected to grow at about 4% 
per year over the next five years. Capacity additions will match this growth52. 
PHA: The high value of the PHA is based not only in the bioBased production process itself, but also in its biodegradable nature, 
as seen in following picture (Figure 1). Biobased polymers such as PHA are generally more sustainable materials than their 
petrochemical-based counterparts, and, as previously mentioned, they can be produced from a wide range of renewable 
resources. The actual market segments of PHA consist in flexible packaging and mulch films (used in blends), and some niche 
markets in consumer goods (cosmetic packagings, horticulture pots, biodegradable cutlery, etc.). The most representative PHAs 
are polyhydroxybutyrate (PHB) and poly hydroxyvalerate (PHV) and its copolymer (PHBV), with physical properties similar to 
polyethylene and polypropylene. In both markets, besides its physical properties, PHA provides biodegradability. This is 
particularly adapted to these markets where the product shelf-life is low. In particular in the case of mulch films, a major advantage 
arises in this case in addition to having the advantages of a traditional plastic film: after use there is no need of collecting the film. 
In 2014, the global production capacities of bioplastics amounted to 1.7 million tons.  
 
Figure 2.1d Production of PHA in 2014. 
This translates to approximately 680,000 hectares of land. The key players in the PHA market are Biomatera, Biomer, Bio -On 
SROL, Kaneka corporation, Meredian Ins., Metabolix Inc, Newlight Technologies, LIC, PHA industrial S.A., Polyferm Canada Inc., 
Shenzhen Ecomann Biotech Co Ltd, Tianan Biologic Materials Co and Tianiin Greenbio Materials Co Ltd. PHA could be used in 
various applications, but the targeted markets in the project are flexible packaging, consumer goods and agriculture/horticulture. 
The actual potential market size can then be estimated at around 80 ,000 tons: 35 ,000 tonnes in flexible packagings (partial

substitution of PLA and PLA blends, starch blends and other biodegradable), 35 ,000 tons in agriculture/horticultu re (partial 
substitution of starch-blends) and 10,000 tons in consumer goods. Due to their properties, scl-and mcl- PHA should in particular 
be a good substitute to PBAT, a fossil-based biopolymer currently used for improving softness and processability of starch-based 
and PLA-based blends. PBAT has today a global production capacity of around 80,000 tons per year. The growth rate from 2014

Biocomposites: Composite materials as used e.g. in the construction, automotive and chemical industries, combine attributes 
from two of more different materials in one combined application. Examples are the combination of (bio)plastics with natural 
materials/fibers coming from wood, wheat or starch. The composite materials are forming a rapidly growing market with a market 
value for WPC alone of 4000 M € per year. The total packaging market in Europe is approximately 130 billion €. Over 50% of this 
market is biobased (paper, board, wood) and 31% is plastic. The major plastic packaging material is PE-based (56%) for mostly 
films, wraps, and bags but also some bottles and containers (HD-PE) and smaller amounts of PP (19%), PET (11%), PS (10%) 
and PVC (4%) are used. For packaging, very few composite materials are currently available. However the possibilities are 
extremely high since composites potentially can com bine the favourable attributes of various compounds, e.g. the light weight 
and strength of plastic with the abundant availability and flexibility of paper and board.    
Fertilisers: Segment of solid biofertilisers is more competitive with traditional mineral and organic-mineral fertilisers, with greater 
size but lower value added. Regarding liquid biofertilisers, microalgae grown on AD liquid and gaseous streams will be used a s 
protein source for aminoacids product ion via hydrolysis, to be used as main component of liquid biofertilisers. Regarding liquid 
biofertilisers in general and based in aminoacids and peptids in particular, are a fast growing but smaller market but with much 
higher value added products, design ated typically for ferti rrigation and fruit and vegetables intensive crops. The Market of 
biofertiliser in 2014 can be seen in picture 2.1f. The increasing awareness regarding health and wellness, the growing organic 
food industry, and the significant increase in the price of chemicals have played a huge role in providing the necessary boost to 
the global biofertilizers industry. Global Biofertilizers Market is expected to reach USD 1.88 Billion by 2020 at a CAGR of 14.0% 
from 2015 to 2020. The phosphate -solubilizing segment is the fastest -growing market in the biofertilizers industry across the 
globe. 
The market was dominated by North America in 2014 and accounted for the largest share in the total biofertilisers market. Asia-
Pacific is projected to be the fastest-growing market for between 2015 and 2020 due to the increasing prices of chemical fertilizers 
and increasing food demand due to population growth. In terms of type, the nitrogen fixing biofertilisers seg ment is projected to 
be the largest market for biofertilisers; whereas in terms of crop -type, cereals & grains is projected to be the largest market for 
biofertilisers in 2014 (Figure 2.1g). The cyanobacteria segment is projected to be the largest market f or biofertilisers by 2020 in 
terms of microorganisms. Furthermore, by application, seed treatment is projected to be the largest market for biofertilisers  in 
between 2015 and 2020. The biofertilisers market is characterized by intense competition due to the presence of a large number 
of both large and small -scale firms. Expansions, acquisitions, agreements and new product launches are the key strategies 
adopted by market players to ensure their growth in the market.
