Brief description of the organization:  
Wageningen University & Research (Wageningen UR) is the umbrella organization comprising legal entities Wageningen 
University and Stichting Wageningen Research. The partner entity is Wageningen Research. Wageningen UR explores the 
potential of nature to improve the quality of life. With approximately 30 locations around the world, 6,000 staff members and 
9,000 students, Wageningen UR is a world leader in the biobased economy domain. An integral way of working and 
cooperation between the exact sciences and t he technological and social disciplines are the key to its approach. 
Wageningen UR partners in developing chemicals, materials, fuels & energy that use biomass instead of fossil resources 
and in the creation and production of healthy and tasty food. Our in -depth knowledge on developing truly sustainable 
biomass valorisation chains (from raw materials through refining to marketable end -products) for food and non -food 
applications drives our approach. Together with our customers, Wageningen UR develops production processes, biobased 
materials, and full sustainable biomass valorisation strategies that will transform the current economy into a sustainable 
bioeconomy. With innovative technologies, Wageningen UR turns both primary crops and residues from the food industry, 
agriculture and natural infrastructure into marketable products and energy: human food, animal feed, chemicals, materials, 
fuels, power and heat. The Institute Food & Biobased Research (FBR) will represent Wageningen Research in this project 
with its fermentation and microalgae expertise in microbial production of medium and long-chain-length (MCFA/LCFA) fatty 
acids and medium chain length (mcl) PHA’s and development of industrial applications for these components. 
Role in the Project: 
Wageningen UR will establish fermentation processes using Cryptococcus sp. for long-chain fatty acid (LCFA) based on the 
existing volatile fatty acid (VFA) stream and will establish a fermentation process for medium -chain length poly -hydroxy 
alkanoate (mcl-PHA) production using the VFA’s and ethanol as substrates and pure cultures of Pseudomonas putida as 
production organism. Role of Wageningen UR includes the participation in WPs 1, 3, 5, 6, 7 and 8. 
Scientific team:  
Dr. Ana Lopez Contreras (F) is a senior scientist at Wageningen Research. She is an expert on the production of fuels and 
chemicals by fermentation of biomass resources and has participated and directed at FBR -WR several projects on 
bioalcohol production, including Isobutanol Platform Rotterdam (IBPR, 2012 -2015), BIOCORE (2010 -2014) and 
SEABIOPLAS (2013 -2015), and several Dutch national projects (TKI - Small scale biorefinery 2014 -2016). She has 
published >20 peer-reviewed scientific articles, 3 book chapters and many reports on biotechnology for fuel and chemical 
production, and has successfully directed 3 PhD thesis and currently supervises 2 PhD students. She is a regular reviewer 
for high impact factor journals in her field. 
Prof. Dr. Jeroen Hugenholtz ( M) is a senior scientist and expertise leader at Wageningen Research. He is an expert in 
(food) fermentation and microbial physiology, especially of anaerobic microorganisms and has been active for over 30 years 
in applied research and industrial R&I (Dutch dairy industry, Coca -Cola, Corbion/Purac) in the area of food and ingredient 
production. He has coordinated and participated in many Dutch and European Public Private Partnerships such as the 
Kluyver Centre, the Wageningen Centre for Food Sciences, TI Food & Nutrition, NutraCells, SysMO, etc. He has published 
over 200 peer-reviewed papers and is author of 25 patents in the area of fermentation. He also holds a part -time chair in 
Industrial Molecular Microbiology at the University of Amsterdam.  
Dr. Frits de Wolf (M) is a senior scientist, project leader, and project acquisition lead at Wageningen Research. He is an 
expert in biophysical chemistry and microbial production of proteins and biopolyme rs including PHA, with additional 
backgrounds in neurophysiology, photosynthesis research and biomembrane biophysics. He has been active for more than 
27 years in applied research, part of which as manager of a group of 30 people, and has acquired and led numerous 
research projects, either entirely funded by industrial clients (including projects amounting to several M€), or with public 
cofunding from national funds or the EU. In addition, he participated in fundamentally scientific projects including an ER C 
Advanced Grant 267254 (BioMate). He has published more than 75 papers in peer -reviewed high- or top-rating journals 
(H27) and 9 patent applications. He co-supervised 14 PhD students.

PARTNER 10 
Name: Centro de Investigaciones 
Energéticas Medioambientales y

Brief description of the organization:  
CIEMAT (Centro de Investigaciones Energéticas, Medioambientales y Tecnológicas) is a Public Research Institution 
depending on the Spanish Ministry of Economy and Competitiveness. Main objectives of CIEMAT are to promote and 
execute research,  innovation and technical development in the energy sector. CIEMAT research is conducted in 
cooperation with other institutions and organisations in joint R&D&I projects, providing specialized technical services, 
education and training. Ciemat´s employees are around 1,326 people, technologically and geographically diversified in 
several research Centres in Spain, with central offices and a great part of the facilities located in Madrid. 
The Biofuels Unit is attached to the Renewable Energies Department of C IEMAT, whose and the main objective is to 
perform R&D projects in the renewable energies area, both in resources evaluation and conversion technology 
development. The Biofuels Unit performs R&D projects in the area of bioconversion processes to transform lignocellulosic 
biomass into bioproducts and biofuels such as bioethanol, by means of efficient and low cost biotechnological routes, 
facilitating that these processes can be adopted by industry. The team is composed of scientists such as analytic chemists, 
biochemists, and microbiologists, with specialization in the different areas involved in the biotechnological conversion of 
lignocellulose into bioproducts and bioethanol. 
In the last years, significant advances in the different steps of the conversion process, i.e., pretreatment, enzymatic 
hydrolysis, and yeast fermentation have been attained, based on large research activity on different lignocellulosic 
materials. It hasTh ose advances allowed the team addressing the use of lignocellulose in a wider approach in order to 
obtain a sugar platform and valuable streams for the production of a variety of bioproduct/biofuels, within the biorefinery 
concept. The experience of the group members is shown by the publications and granted projects achieved. 
 
Role in the Project: 
CIEMAT will be focused on adapting/improving the PERSEO bioethanol production process to the new feedstock by 
optimizing pretreatment, enzymatic hydrolysis and fermentation steps. Apart from sugar recovery and ethanol yield, energy 
efficiency ratio, mass balance and toxic compounds generation will be used for the estimation of the optimized parameters. 
CIEMAT will also assist IMECAL in the analysis of the fractions generated in the demo trials. Role of CIEMAT includes the 
participation in WPs 1, 2, 5, 6, 7 and 8. 
Scientific team:  
Dr. Mercedes Ballesteros (F) is Head of the Biofuels Unit at CIEMAT since 2000. She is Ph.D. in Biology by the Autonoma 
University and Master in Biotechnology by Complutense University of Madrid and has more than 30 years’ experience in 
the field of biomass utilization by biological processes for energy purposes, mainly in developing technologies to produce 
bioethanol from lignocellulosic biomass. She has authored about 90 papers and 5 chapters in technical books and is co -
author of 3 patents. 
Dr. Ignacio Ballesteros (M) is a Senior Researcher at the Biofuels Unit of CIEMATand obtained his PhD degree in Biology 
at the University of Alcalá de Henares in 2000. He has large expertise in biological processes for biomass valorization in 
the field of enzymatic hydrolysis and fermentation as well as in biomass pre-treatment, developinghis entire research career 
within the Department of Energy's Renewable Energy Unit atCIEMAT. He is co -author of 35 scientific papers and co -
inventor of 2 patents. 
Dr. José Miguel Oliva Domínguez  (M), S enior Researcher at Biofuels Unit of Ciemat. He has a PhD in Biology by 
University Complutense of Madrid. His main activity is focused on the production of second generation bioethanol from 
lignocellulosic biomass. He has a broad experience in all steps involved in the ethanol production process, mainly in steam 
explosion pretreatment, enzymatic hydrolysis and simultaneous saccharification and fermentation process using 
thermotolerant yeasts. He has published more than 30 papers in SCI journals, numerous  conference contributions and is 
coauthorof 3 patents. He has participated in numerous National and International projects of R&D. He has also 
cosupervised 1 PhD and 3 Master Thesis.

PARTNER 11 
Name: Visum Ltd. (Terminated) 
Web page: www.seeingnewdata.com 
Type: SME 
Country: Ireland  
Brief description of the organization:  
VISUM is an optical engineering company from Dublin that specializes in the development and commercialization of Near 
Infrared Spectroscopy (NIRS) -based portable and inline analyzers and measurement systems for process monitoring and 
quality control applications in the food, pharmaceutical and chemical applications. The company was established in 2014 and 
currently employs 9 staff from its facilities in Nexus UCD (technology park at the University College Dublin campus). In 2016 , 
the company launched the VISUMPalm (a handheld NIR measurement instrument) and has secured first sales. An integration 
platform (VisumNIR) for the inline version is currently under development in cooperation with early adopter clients from the 
food processing industry (dairy, meat and olive oil) and pharmaceutical industries. VISUM Ltd. is an SME Champion-awarded 
a Phase 1 and a subsequent Phase 2 Grant under H2020’s SME Instrument. 
Role in the Project:  
VISUM will participate in WP2 where they will test novel advanced sensors for mo nitoring selected process parameters and 
will integrate them at plant level together with a bespoke control system. They will also collaborate in the study of the relevant 
parameters to be analysed in order to select the most suitable sensor technologies.  
VISUM will also develop data mining tools to produce relevant decision support tools for optimally controlling production based 
on relevant process data.  
VISUM will also participate in WP1, supporting the work on preliminary actions for biorefinery design which will feed into WP2.  
VISUM will also take part in WP6, in the environmental, economic and regulatory assessments from the monitoring at plant 
scale point of view and will participate in WP7 by actively engaging in exploitation and d issemination tasks. Role of VISUM  
includes the participation in WPs 5 and 8. 
Scientific team:  
DR. TIMOTHY KEHOE (MALE) PhD in Engineering, University College Cork, Ireland - developed an IR photometric 
measurement system for thermal diffusivity in anisotropic materials.  Masters in Optoelectronics, Queens University Belfast. 
Designed an Echelle spectrometer while based at An dor Technology Ltd.  Worked at Tyndall National Institute, Cork, and 
Catalan Institute of Nanotechnology, Barcelona, developing diffractive optical and acousto -optical metrology techniques for 
nanotechnology applications, in particular for characterision of nanoscale structures fabricated by nanoimprint lithography and 
directed self-assembly. Other research activities: quantification of ordering in self -assembled structures by image analysis 
techniques and optical sensors using hydrogel materials. Tim is the VISUM Palm Product Manager, as well as the Technical 
Lead at VISUM Ltd. and he brings vast optical integration and device instrumentation expertise.  
DR. SEVERINE PHILIPPE (FEMALE)  graduated with a 1st class Hons. B.Sc. in Applied Sciences (Physics and Ph ysics 
Technology) from Dublin Institute of Technology, Ireland, in 2002 and received her PhD in Physics from Trinity College Dublin 
in 2007, for the study of polarisation effects in semiconductor optical amplifiers (SOA). She went on to work on the development 
and characterization of low noise SOAs, as well as THz generation by multimode lasers and their detection using four wave 
mixing in SOA, both in the Semiconductor Photonics Group, Trinity College Dublin, and the School of Electronic Engineering,

PARTNER 12 
Name: NaturePlast 
Web page: http://www.natureplast.eu/

Country: 
FRANCE 
 
Brief description of the organization: 
Natureplast Company was founded in December 2006 and is specialized in supporting bioplastics applications development 
through its activities: 
• Raw materials distribution: hundreds of different references of biobased and / or biodegradable materials (PLA, PHAs, 
PBS, starch based materials, etc.) available with worldwide network. 
• Services such as project engineering, technical support, technical survey, training, etc.  
• And Research & Development through its 100% owned daughter company Biopolynov: our compounding facility allows 
us to develop and produce compounds with improved properties depending of our customers and applications needs. 
The company’s works usually concerns improving mechanical, processing or thermal properties through the use of several 
raw materials, additives, natural fibers, nanofillers or by-products (or wastes) from different industries.  
In summary, the goal is to help industry to put bioplastics end -products into the market through our access to raw materials 
and our capacity to develop specific compounds with improved properties. 
 
Natureplast and Biopolynov teams are  working for years in bioplastics modification and properties improvement. The team 
gained a strong experience in R&D on bioplastics which permit Biopolynov to answer the best to the customers’ needs. 
Natureplast also has the widest raw material portfolio in Europe and has access to almost all kind of bioplastics and additives 
all around the world.  
Biopolynov has a strong knowledge in the development and production of bioplastics compounds at small industrial scale 
(from 1 to 50 kg). An industrial production line (200 kg/h) is planned to be bought by the end of 2017. 
 
Role in the Project:  
Natureplast’s role will mainly be focused on characterizing the raw polymers (BioPE  and PHA) produced by the consortium, 
and to modify them to improve their processability and properties for the targeted applications. 
Role of Natureplast includes the participation in WPs 1, 5, 6, 7 and 8.

Name: IRIAF (CLAMBER BIOREFINERY)

Brief description of the organization:  
The Regional Institute of Castilla-La Mancha for Agri-Food and Forest Research and Development (IRIAF), which belongs to 
the Ministry of Agriculture and Environment of the Regional Government of Castilla-La Mancha (JCCM), consists of 6 different 
research centres. It developed the CLAMBER Project (Castilla-La Mancha Bio-Economy Region Project) and has extensive 
experience in proceedings related to R&D&i as well as dissemination and training activities related to the agri-food and forest 
sectors.  
Thanks to this Project, which was co-funded with ERDF through the Ministry of Economy and Competitiveness of the National 
Government of Spain (MINECO), the CLAMBER Plant was built in 2015. It is a public demonstrative biorefinery at the service 
of companies and dedicated to scientific research, scale-up experiments and development of new processes and high value 
products from lignocellulosic and fermentable biomass, both residual and cultivated.  
 
Role in the Project: IRIAF will be in charge of the VFA valorisation throughout the conversion of VFA to MCFA and later into 
PHA in a demonstrative scale (bioreactor of 20 m 3). Also, it will treat with a passive system based on constructed wetlands 
the wastewater generated once the microorganisms that accumulate PHA are separ ated. Role of IRIAF includes the 
participation in WPs 1, 3, 6, 7 and 8.

es 
Technical equipment: 
IRIAF’s infrastructure relevant to the proposed work: 
- UP-STREAM: this area aims to transform raw lignocellulosic biomass into a fermentable carbon source or store and adapt 
raw materials (waste or by-products) that can be part of fermentation media. It consists of solid biomass storage (4 x 35 m3), 
mills for herbaceous and woody biomass (200 kg/h), extractor with vapour (3 m3), Two-step Steam Explosion Reactor (400 l, 
up to 21 barg) and solid-liquid separators (15 m3/h). 
- MID-STREAM: this area aims to transform biologically (anaerobic digestion, fermentation, etc.) the carbon source generated 
in Up-stream in different products of interest (biofuels, building blocks, etc.). It consists of Microbiology laboratory for micro 
management (starters, inoculants, etc.), Bioreactors for hydrolysis and anaerobic and aerobic fermentation: 2 x 3 l, 2 x 30 l, 1 
x 300 l, 1 x 3,000 l and 1 x 20,000 l, Systems for sterilization, substrate preparation, addition of sterile reactants, clean ing in 
place, and other utilities. 
- DOWN-STREAM: this ar ea is formed by a series of modules of typical technologies of industrial biotechnology segment 
ability to interface with each other in order to concentrate and purify the products of interest from the remaining fermentation 
broth. It consists of Harvestin g Tanks with capacity to act as a extractor: 1 x 10,000 l (ATEX), 1 x 10,000 l (No ATEX), 1 x 
1,500 l (ATEX) and 1 x 1,500 l (No ATEX), Microfiltration system (1 m3/h) and Centrifugation system (1.5 m3/h) (ATEX). 
- ANAEROBIC DIGESTION: It is a modular and transportable pilot plant for experiments of anaerobic digestion and co -
digestion of solid and liquid organic biomass to produce biogas and bio -fertilizers. It consists of Liquid storage tank (20 m 3), 
Solid storage hopper (10 m 3), Pasteurization tank (1 m 3), Homogenization Tank (3 m 3), Digester (11 m 3), Digestate tank (5 
m3) and Gasometer (10 m3). 
- UTILITIES: this area includes all the necessary services for the operation of the bio -refinery units: power supply, steam 
boilers, process water, cooling water (Tower, chiller, glycol), compressed air and instrumentation and process gases (NH 3, 
N2, O2, CO2). 
- WASTE MANAGEMENT: this area includes the necessary for the treatment and/or storage of liquid and solid wastes 
produced in the biorefinery facilities previous to the discharge or delivery manager, always prioritizing the 3Rs: Reduce, Reuse 
and Recycle. The wastewater treatment is based on a hybrid constructed wetland system with reeds, where the organic 
compounds of the wastewater that could not be used previously are assimilated and generate a new lignocellulosic biomass 
that can be used as a new resource in the plant, closing the cycle. 
- PROCESS CONTROL: this area is a control room where, through a SCADA system, the following activities are carried out: 
processing and monitoring of the process information, remote control devices and settings values in databases system, alarm 
monitoring and deployment of processes, reporting and statistical process data and storage of historical data and trends of 
process variables. 
- ANALYTICAL CONTROL PROCESS: It is a laboratory that supports the activity of the processing and environmental units, 
aimed at providing the necessary techniques to develop most of the assessments and monitoring the operation of the different 
experiments.

Name: NATRUE AISBL

Brief description of the organization:  
NATRUE AISBL is a Brussels -based international non -profit association consisting of natural and organic cosmetics 
manufacturers, and a holder and setter of a private standard in the sector. NATRUE is not a certification body.   
Founded in 2007 by pioneers of the sector including WELEDA AG, WALA Heilmittel GmbH (Dr.Hauschka Skin Care), 
Laverana GmbH & Co. KG, Primavera Life GmbH, NATRUE has currently 49 members spread across 25 countries. 
Adoption of the NATRUE label on certified products currently exceeds 4.700 products across a total of 204 brands. 
NATRUE’s activities can be grouped into three main areas: 
Advocacy: NATRUE works to protect and promote natural and organic cosmetics and their ingredients, assessing the 
impact that emerging or evolving regulations will have on the sector and contributing to the discussions with the European 
institutions, always bearing in mind consumer’s expectations and sectorial needs 
Label: NATRUE helps consumers to identify which products are truly natural and organic in a sector which still lacks a legal 
and harmonized definition. 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 95 
 
 
Research: NATRUE pools resources to investigate scie ntific issues of concern. Being an association, we gather the 
expertise of different companies, institutions and other associations to help the sector position itself with regards to specific 
topics. 
NATRUE is listed on the EU transparency register under the identification number: 75060586073-86. NATRUE is a liaison 
organisation representative providing expert technical opinion to ISO TC/217/WG4 and CEN TC/392 for cosmetics, and is 
a member of the EU Commission’s Working Group on Cosmetic Products (E01302). 
 
Role in the Project:  
The Role of NATRUE AISBL is related to the coordination with supporting member companies from the association which 
includes:  
- Development of packaging concepts and specifications for cosmetic applications in WP5.

In addition to that, the role of NATRUE AISBL includes the participation in WPs 1, 6, 7 and 8.

PARTNER 17 
Name: Agencia Estatal Consejo Superior de 
Investigaciones Científicas 
Web page: www.icp.csic.es  
Type: RTO 
Country: Spain  
Brief description of the organization:  
The CSIC (Spanish National Research Council) is Spain’s largest public research institution, and ranks third among Europe’s 
largest research organization. The CSIC is attached to the Spanish Ministry of Science, Innovation and Universities through 
the State Secretariat for Research, Development and Innovation, and plays a key role in scientific and technological policy in 
Spain and worldwide.  According to its Statute (Article 4), CSIC has 4 main missions: 
- to foster multidisciplinary scientific and technological research 
- Knowledge transfer to industry and society 
- Education and training of scientific and technical staff  
- Creation of Technology Based Companies  
CSIC has 10.940 employees, including 3.764 researchers. CSIC has 120 Institutes spread across the country and covering 
different areas of Science and Technology. 67 of them are fully-owned institutes and 53 are Joint Research Units in partnership 
with other Spanish universities or research institutions. CSIC has also a delegation in Brussels. 
CSIC supports research and training across a wide range of knowledge, from the most basic or fundamental aspects of science 
to the most complex technological developments; from human and social sciences to food science and technology, including 
biology, biomedicine, physics, chemistry and mater ials, natural resources and agricultural sciences. As the third largest 
research organization in Europe, CSIC carries out research in all fields of knowledge, throughout its 123 Institutes distributed 
in eight areas: 
• Humanities and Social Sciences 
• Biology and Biomedicine 
• Natural Resources 
• Agricultural Sciences 
• Physical Science and Technologies 
• Materials Science and Technology 
• Food Science and Technology 
• Chemical Science and Technology 
CSIC produces 20% of the national scientific output (more than 12.000 ISI paper in 2014). CSIC remains the leading patent 
filer among research bodies in Spain with more than 180 patent requests published in 2014. 
CSIC has a broad experience in managing large and singular infrastructures. For instance, CSIC provides services to the entire 
scientific community through management of the Singular Scientific and Technological Infrastructures (ICTS) such as Calar 
Alto Astronomical Observatory (with MaxPlanck), Doñana Biological Station, European Synchrotron Radiat ion Facility, 
Hesperides Ocean Research Vessel, Integrated Micro and Nanoelectronics Clean Room, Juan Carlos I Antarctic Base, Max 
Von Laue-Paul Langevin Institute and Sarmiento de Gamboa Ocean Research Vessel. 
In addition, CSIC has a broad experience in conducting R&D projects funded by national and international public agencies and 
industry. 
CSIC is a major player in the development of the European research area and therefore a significant contributor to the 
European integration process. Within the 7th Framework Programme CSIC has signed 726 actions (including 70 coordinated 
by CSIC). As to the number of projects, CSIC is listed the 1st organisation in Spain and the 4th in Europe within the research 
organizations, with a total FP7 contribution of over 264 million euros (E-CORDA).  
As to the funding obtained by CSIC within each program, the distribution is People 20%, Cooperation 47%, Capacities 8% and 
Ideas 25%. Taking into account the research areas, the most relevant ones in terms of funding have been Phy sical Science 
and Technology and Biology and Biomedicine.  
In H2020 (2014-2018) CSIC has obtained 446 projects with a total EU financial contribution of 181 million euros. As E-CORDA 
points out CSIC is listed the 1st organisation in Spain and the 3rd parti cipant within the research organization by number of 
projects. 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 97 
 
 
CSIC is a major player in the ERC programme with a total of 87 projects signed as Host Institution in all areas of knowledge.  
CSIC is also an active member in Knowledge and Innovation Communities (KIC), such as Raw Materials and Food of the 
European Institute for Innovation and Technology (EIT). 
The Group for Energy and Sustainable Chemistry from the ‘ Instituto de Catálisis y Petroleoquímica’ (ICP) of CSIC is located 
in Madrid. It comprises 10 staff researchers, 5 postdoc students and several PhD and Master students. It has a long experience 
in the field of applied and basic catalysis (heterogeneous, homogeneous, electrocatalysis and photocatalysis) and catalytic 
process for clean energy and sustainable chemistry. In particular, it has a great deal of experience in the field of C1 chemistry, 
including H2 production from CH4 and syngas transformation into hydrocarbons (liquid fuels, short chain olefins…) and/or 
oxygenates (methanol, DME etc). The group has participated in several EU and National projects related with these topics 
Role in the Project:  
CSIC is the partner in the project in charge of designing and building a pilot plant for bioethylene production from bio ethanol. 
The pilot p lant will produce Ethylene with a goal to validate that the produced ethanol is suitable for sustainable ethylene 
derivative chemicals. Role of CSIC includes the participation in the following WPs: 2, 5, 6, 7 and 8. 
 
Scientific team:

Technical equipment: 
CSIC team has the following techniques in catalysts preparation in their own labs, or are fully accessible in their institute. For 
the synthesis of catalysts, we have two lines of synthesis at controlled atmospheres (Schlenk), 2 rotary evaporators, ovens 
and reactors for thermal synthesis, autoclaves for thermal synthesis, one glove box and other minor equipment. As for the 
characterization CSIC have (or have access to) Infrared spectrometers, Raman spectrometer, XPS, XRD (with in situ chamber 
for treatment of samples under controlled atmosphere and temperature), Chemisorption of gases, BET, ATG, HRTEM/STEM. 
In addition, they have the possibility of using synchrotron facilities for XAS (EXAFS and XANES) and XPS analyses and access 
to nuclear reactors for Pow er neutron diffraction. All of the above techniques will be available for the sake of the project.   In 
addition, in situ characterization using DRIFT cells are available. CSIC is well equipped for the evaluation of the catalytic  
activity. We have two Microactivity catalytic reactors, consisting in a fixed bed reactor and the possibility to feed up to 4 gases 
and liquids. Temperature is controlled by a thermocouple in the catalytic bed, from r.t. to 700 °C. The reactor is integrated  
within a hot box, with ma ximum temperature of 180º C. A pressure control system allows working at pressures up to 90 bar. 
Several gases and liquid can be fed to the reactors. Product analysis and quantification is done by gas chromatography. We 
have two on line Varian CP -3800 gas chromatograph equipped with a Hayesep Q packed column (2 m length, 0.32 mm 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 99 
 
 
diameter) connected to a thermal conductivity detector (TCD) and an Rtx-1 capillary (60 m length 0.25 mm diameter) column 
connected to a flame ionization detector (FID). Adapted to cryogenic cooling. 
CSIC team has access and utilises industry-leading software for the simulation and optimisation of chemical processes, Aspen 
Plus software engineering (V10 (36.0.0249)), this software will be used for the basic engineering design of the bioethanol 
dehydration module to be constructed and operated in PERSEO installations.

1. Hypera®-Hyperspectral Imaging (HSI) System for on -line foreign body detection and chemical composition analysis 
(H2020- SMEInst, Grant Agreement no: 726572, 2016 -2018). IRIS is further developing its inline HSI monitoring produ ct 
line and validating it in the food industry.  To date the system has been applied commercially for inline pizza quality 
assessment (topping distribution and absence of plastic flakes). Using HSI, not only the shape or position but also the 
chemical composition and hidden matter can be unveiled. Using Artificial Intelligence algorithms (chemometrics), we convert 
the monitored outputs into meaningful information much beyond that can be obtained with conventional vision systems. Our 
HSI system prevents out-of-spec processes or product recalls.  
2. ProPAT- Robust and affordable process control technologies for improving standards and optimising industrial operations 
(H2020- SPIRE, Grant Agreement no: 637232, 2014-2018). IRIS is leading the development of a global PAT control platform 
integrating novel low cost smart sensors, such as MEMS-NIR spectroscopic and granulometric analysers, which together 
with chemometric tools and predictive software, is being validated to optimise the performance of batch and continuous 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 102 
 
 
processing industries. As initial target appl ications, the milling of minerals, ceramics, metals, mixing and granulation of 
pharma products and polymerization of resins are optimized both in terms of process efficiency, and output quality. 
3. ShareBox- Secure Management Platform for Shared Process Resou rces, Grant Agreement no: 680843, Funding 
programme: H2020 -SPIRE-06-2015. IRIS is developing the central cloud platform that integrates a number of smart 
modules for supporting and optimising synergies in the use of shared resources and waste valorisation between companies.

Industry4.0 paradigm, and allows the shift of industrial users towards the circular economy.

Agreement no: 315743 FP7, R4SME, Demonstration action Completed). IRIS participated in the process upscaling and 
optimisation, among other via simulation and monitoring, for multilayer films with improved recyclability by design. 
5. OPTIJECT- A novel spectroscopic instrument for in -line monitoring during injection moulding (FP7, R4SMEs, Grant 
agreement no.: 262623, 2010 -2012). IRIS has implemented NIR and UV -VIS spectroscopy -based in line monitoring 
systems for the polymer melt stream within the nozzle of injection machines. In particular, IRIS delivered probes able  to 
withstand outstandingly high pressure and temperatures up to 350ºC. The system was demonstrated to provide information 
normally only available to plastic converters via laborious off line QC tests like material humidity down to ppm level or 
polymer degradation, as well as to allow optimising colour and colourant concentration in real time. When coupled with case 
based reasoning models, it allows decision support for real time process optimization thereby maximizing yield and 
minimizing the scrap generated.

6. ADDENDUM 1. BUSINESS CASES 
 
BIOETHANOL as Building block 
 
1. Value proposition. 
Ethanol is a colourless, volatile, flammable liquid produced by yeast fermentation of carbohydrates, or synthetically, by hydration 
of ethylene. It is used chiefly as a transport fuel or fuel additive (replacing fossil gasoline), with other applications bei ng used as 
heating fuel, a solvent , a chemical industry feedstock, and in beverages, antiseptics and medicines. Bioethanol is the largest 
biofuel with an estimated worldwide production of 100 billion litres in 2013, and with an expected production of 150 billion litres in 
202063.  The growing market for bioethanol as a biofuel has also been accompanied by an increasingly interest as a useful 
platform molecule for the production of value-added chemicals (Figure 6.1). 
Bioethanol is mainly produced by yeast fermentation of sugar-rich and starch-rich biomass like sugarcane (Brazil), maize (North 
America) or cereals (Europe). The industrial production of ethanol from second -generation lignocellulose biomass is rapidly 
developing, with many projects being developed in the US and EU. 
 
 
Figure 6.1. Ethanol as chermical building block (adapted from ref.64) 
 
2. Existing alternatives 
93% of total ethanol produced worldwide comes from bio based methods (yeast fermentation), while only 7% is fossil ethanol 
(catalytic hydration of ethylene)65. 
 
 
3. Price per unit 
Market price evolution of ethanol during last year is shown in Figure 6.2.  
 
63 (OECD-FAO, 2014) http://ethanolproducer.com/articles/11264/oecd-fao-outlook-report-predicts-growth-in-ethanol-production 
64 http://www.e4tech.com/project/from-the-sugar-platform-to-biofuels-and-biochemical/  
65 http://www.e4tech.com/project/from-the-sugar-platform-to-biofuels-and-biochemical/  

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 108 
 
 
 
Figure 6.2. Ethanol price evolution in 2015-2016 period (Values in US $/GAL)66. 
 
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plan. 
Bioethanol from MSW can be feasible in installations treating 40,000 – 50,000 tonnes of MSW per year, depending mainly of the 
OFMSW quality, and the bioethanol yields obtained in the process (around 100 -130 liters of bioethanol from tonne of dry MSW 
tonne). 
 
5. Market segment 
Main current use of bioethanol is as transport biofuel or fuel additive for gasoline. Bioethanol has also been used in the beverage 
industry and as renewable chemical building block for the production of ethylene, propylene, diethyl ether , 1,3 -butadiene, 
acetaldehyde, ethyl acetate, etc.67 (Figure 6.3). 
 
 
Figure 6.3. Worldwide production and use of ethanol68 
 
6. Market size 
Global bioethanol market volume was 100 billion litres in 201469. 
 
7. Growth rate 
It is expected a bioethanol production of 150 billion litres in 202070, which means an increase of 50% compared to current market 
volumes.  
 
8. Market trends 
Currently bioethanol is produced from edible biomass, mainly from sugar in Brazil and starch in USA (Figure 6.4). 
 
66 http://www.tradingeconomics.com/commodity/ethanol/forecast  
67 Sun,J and Wang, Y,. Recent advances in catalytic conversion of ethanol to chemicals. ACS Catalysis. 2014, 4, 1078−1090 
68 Angelici, C.; Weckhuysen, B. M.; Bruijnincx, P. C. A.; ChemSusChem 2013, 6, 1595. 
69 http://www.e4tech.com/project/from-the-sugar-platform-to-biofuels-and-biochemical/ 
70 (OECD-FAO, 2014) http://ethanolproducer.com/articles/11264/oecd-fao-outlook-report-predicts-growth-in-ethanol-production 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 109 
 
 
 
 
Figure 6.4. Production of bioethanol per country or region in 2013 71. 
 
However, due to the competition with food and concerns about ecological systems, recent research has shifted towards bioethanol 
production from non-food biomass feedstocks (lignocellullosic materials from agricultural, industrial and urban wastes), which are 
low cost and abundantly available sources. Second generation lignocellulosic ethanol is near commercialisation, with first -of-a-
kind commercial plants built or currently under construction, and is therefore within the scope of the study, although a great deal 
is known about this industry already, and therefore less effort is afforded it compared to more novel pathways to different f uels 
and chemicals.  
 
9. Barriers and risks 
The main bar riers facing bioethanol production are: the high cost and energy use in product separation, the stability of yeast 
strains and the low productivity due to substrate/product inhibition and slow degradation of substrates. These barriers have been 
mitigated by the latest advances in the use of more efficient downstream processing techniques, new strains with improved 
stability and higher productivities and the efficient integration of pre-treatment with fermentation steps72. 
 
10. Business approach 
IMECAL and CIEMAT have developed a biotechnological patented technology (European Patent EPO 2112226, USA Patent 
8399228 B2 and international PCT WIPO 099038) for obtaining second generation bioethanol from OFMSW (PERSEO 
Bioethanol®). The commercialization rights belong to IMECAL. The bioethanol obtained, currently used as a biofuel, will be used 
as chemical building block for the conversion to bioethylene and subsequent polymerisation to bioPE. 
 
 
BIO ETHYLENE  
 
1. Value proposition. 
Ethylene is primarily a monomer used as a feedstock in the manufacture of polymer plastics, fibers and other organic chemicals 
that are ultimately consumed in the packaging, transportation and construction industries and in a multitude of industrial an d 
consumer markets. Nondurable or consumable end uses —in particular, packaging —make up more than half of ethylene 
derivative consumption worldwide. One plastic resin in particular, polyethylene (PE), accounts for the majority of ethylene 
consumption. Ethylene is one of the largest -volume chemicals worldwide 73, with a diverse derivative portfolio, including 
nondurable and durable end uses (Figure 6.5). 
 
 
71 http://ethanolrfa.org/pages/World-Fuel-Ethanol-Production  
72 http://www.e4tech.com/project/from-the-sugar-platform-to-biofuels-and-biochemical/ 
73 https://www.ihs.com/products/ethylene-chemical-economics-handbook.html 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 110 
 
 
 
Figure 6.5. Derivative chemicals and polymers from ethylene (adapted from ref. 74) 
 
2. Existing alternatives 
Fossil ethylene is derived from either modifying natural gas (a methane, ethane, propane mix) or from the catalytic cracking of 
crude oil75. Ethylene from renewable raw materials (bio-ethylene) is made by catalytic dehydratation of bio-ethanol76. Producing 
ethylene from bioethanol is the only commercial solution for biobased and sustainable downstream production. 
 
3. Price per unit 
In India and Brazil, where bioethanol feedstocks (typically sugarcane) are relatively inexpensive and easily accessib le, bio -
ethylene production costs are closer to fossil ones. In Brazil and India they are typically 1,200 $/tonne bio-ethylene, and in China 
(using sweet sorghum feedstocks) are around 1,700 $/tonne. In the United States, bioethylene costs (from corn) are reported at 
about 2,000 $/tonne, and in Europe (from sugar beet) around 2,600 $/tonne. Lignocellulose -based bio-ethylene production is 
estimated at 1,900-2,000 $/tonne in the US77. 
 
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
Bioethylene can be feasible in any capacity. However, it depends on the comparison to petroleum based ethylene price, and 
economy of scale has to be taken into account. In general, capacities over 40  KTA are mostly successful and feasible plants, 
depending on the downstream products78. 
 
5. Market segment 
Ethylene is mainly used as a monomer for the production of plastics: PE, PET, PEG, PVC and PS. PE, the largest ethylene 
derivative market, accounted for over 60% of global ethylene consumption in 201479. Markets include film, packaging, containers 
and articles for home and light industrial use. The next-largest derivative market is ethylene oxide for ethylene glycol used primarily 
to produce PET (used in polyethylene terephthalate fibers, bottles and other goods). In 2014, ethylene oxide accounts for 15% of 
total world consumption. Ethylene dichloride (primarily for vinyl chloride and polyvinyl chloride resin) accounted for 10% of  the

world ethylene market in 2014. Apart from its use as monomer for plastics, ethylene has also been used as fruit ripening agent in 
agriculture80. 
  
6. Market size 
Total worldwide production of ethylene was 127 million tons in 2014, being one of the most consumed chemicals 81. Figure 6.6 
shows world consumption of ethylene per region. 
 
 
Figure 6.6. World consumption of ethylene per region (2014)82 
 
Braskem is the first commercial producer of bioethylene from bioethanol, utilising Brazil’s traditional strengths in producing 
large volumes of low cost sugarcane ethanol for transport. The ethylene produced is processed directly to bioPE83. Annual 
bioethylene production capacity is up to 200 ktpa84. 
  
7. Growth rate 
During 2009–2014, world ethylene consumption grew at an average rate of almost 4.5% per year, while capacity increased at a 
slower rate of about 3.5%. Ethylene consumption is expected to grow at about 4% per year over the next five years. Capacity 
additions will match this growth85. 
The global production capacity of bio-based ethylene to produce bioPE is estimated to be able to reach 840 ktonnes by 2020 86, 
quadrupling current capacities.  
The improved GHG and fossil energy use performance of bio-based PE, together with increasing focus on sustainability and more 
stringent environmental regulations and targets, is anticipated to drive demand for bio-based ethylene. 
 
8. Market trends 
Bioethylene has so far been successfully produced in Brazil, where ethanol is relatively inexpensive and easily available. Cheaper 
production of second generation bioethanol from alternative sources (lignocellulosic ethanol from industrial, agricultural and urban 
wastes) is expected to expand the bioethylene production market to different regions.  
When it comes to bio-plastics (the biggest end use of bio-ethylene), according to BCCC research the market size will grow from 
$387.6 billion in 2013, to $700.7 billion in 2018. Demand for biobased products is growing due to: 
- Corporate social responsibility 
- Sustainability by manufacturers 
- Petroleum dependency 
- Environmental responsibility 
- Demand for renewable 
- Brand value

9. Barriers and risks 
Barriers and risks to the bio-based ethylene market expanding are associated with the bioPE market, since it is the main derivative 
product (see bioPE business case). 
 
10. Business approach 
Bioethylene will be produced from catalytic dehydration of second generation bioethanol from OFMSW. In this case the business 
model is based on the technology licensing for the catalytic conversion from bioethanol to bioethylene. Bioethylene will be u sed 
to produce bioPE and also as a fruit ripening agent.  IP rights on the associated technologies will belong to IMECAL (bioethanol 
from MSW) and GI dynamics (Bioehylene technology). 
 
 
MEDIUM-CHAIN FATTY ACIDS (C8-C14) AS BUILDING BLOCK 
 
1. Value proposition. 
Medium-chain fatty acids with carbon chain lengths varying from 8 to 14 have a broad range of applications as lubricants, creams, 
soaps, antimicrobials, and edible oils. They are not constituents of fossil fuels and relative complicated chemic al processes are 
needed to make them from longer-chain fatty acids. Coconut and palm kernel oil are the natural sources for these fatty acids with 
coconut oil consisting for almost 50% of lauric acid (C12), 16% myristic acid (C14), 8% decanoic acid (C10) and 7% caprylic acid 
(C8). Both coconut and palm are under extreme pressure as source for these MCFA’s because of their additional use as food 
materials (competition for food) and their high impact on the environment (land/water use, agricultural waste). Alternative sources 
that can be produced using more sustainable technologies, such as yeast and microalgae, are in increasing demand. 
Caprylic acid is a strong antimicrobial/antifungal and is died as ingredient to soaps and cosmetic preparations exactly for that 
purpose 
Decanoic acid, as sodium salt, is widely applied in soap. In addition is used in perfumes, lubricants and plastics. 
Lauric acid is mainly applied as cleansing agent (soap) because of its surfactant properties and strong antimicrobial activity. 
Myristic acid is applied, usually as ester, in cosmetics as a surfactant, cleansing, opacifying and thickening agent.       
 
2. Existing alternatives 
Nowadays, coconut oil and palm kernel oil are the major sources for lauric acid. Myristic acid is isolated f rom coconut, nutmeg 
and babassu palm. Caprylic acid and ecanoic acid are isolated from coconut oil and goat milk fat. The major end-users of these 
MCFA’s are large Food, Personal Care and Chemical companies such as Unilever, Proctor & Gamble, AKZONobel and Mitsui. 
  
3. Price per unit 
Caprylic acid is sold for prices ranging from 3 to 12 $ kg-1 depending on its purity and application (in cosmetics/soaps or foods). 
Decanoic acid is generally sold in the price range of 4-6 $ kg-1 to the flavour and fragrance industry. 
Lauric acid market price varies from 3 € kg-1 for detergent application to 5-10 € kg-1 for cosmetic application to > 20 € kg-1 for food 
application. 
Myristic acid market price varies from 3-5 € kg-1 for applications in skin oil to over 10 € kg-1 for food applications. 
 
4. Market segment 
Caprylic acid is widely used in the cosmetic, food and feed industry for its antimicrobial activity. 
Decanoic acid is applied in the chemical industry for its lubrication properties, but also in the cosmetic industry as lubricant but 
also because of its flavour properties. 
Lauric acid, as sodium salt, is soap and as such is applied in most detergents, soaps and other cleansing agents used in the 
Home & Personal Care and Cosmetic industry. It is also m ore-and-more applied as antimicrobial ingredient in Food & Feed 
Industry. 
Myristic acid is mostly applied in the Cosmetic Industry as a surfactant, cleansing, opacifying and thickening agent.  Has also 
emulsifying properties. 
 
5. Market size 
The total fatty acid market size is expected to hit 12 ,000 M€ in 2017, about 40% of this market in the personal care (detergent, 
soap, cosmetics) sector. The current market size for caprylic acid is approximately 2.5 M€. The market size for decanoic acid is 
approximately 50 M€, for lauric acid approximately 2,000 M€ and for myristic acid approximately 600 M€. 
 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 113 
 
 
6.   Growth rate 
The fatty acid market size is expected to double in the next 5-6 years in both the cosmetic and food applications. 
 
7. Market trends 
Growth of coconut and palm trees for its production of fatty acids/oils is steadily growing in tropical countries. This has resulted in 
massive reduction of natural tropical forest areas and general negative sentiment about consumer products based on these crops. 
Alternative sources such as (micro-)algae and also several production processes based on synthetic biology/genetic engineering 
are rapidly penetrating the market.  
 
8. Barriers and risks 
The main barrier for the microbial MCFA market is the price difference with the coconut/palm-derived fatty acids.  
 
9. Business approach 
MCFA’s will be isolated from microalgae used for CO2/NH3-stripping of the biogas and by growth of various Cryptococcus variants 
on the VFA produced by the anaerobic digesters.  
 
 
PHA 
 
1. Value proposition  
The high value of the PHA is based not only in the bioBased  production process itself, but also in its biodegradable nature, as 
seen in following picture (Figure 6.7). Biobased polymers such as PHA are generally more sustainable materials than their 
petrochemical-based counterparts,  and,  as  previously  mentioned,  they can  be  produced  from  a  wide  range  of  renewable 
resources. 
 
 
Figure 6.7.  Procedure and biodegradability of plastic materials 
 
 
Additionally, the PHAs represent the only class of thermoplastic polymers which are converted directly by microorganisms from 
feedstock without necessity of additional polymerisation steps. Moreover due to its characteristics the PHAs can replace some of 
the most common fossil-fuel plastics, such as: PET (Polyethylene terephthalate), LDPE (Low Density Polyethylene), HDPE (High 
Density Polyethylene), and PP (Polypropylene) such is consider in the image below from Lund et al. These four types of plastics 
currently represent more than 50% of worldwide plastics production (as depicted in Figure 6.8).  
 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 114 
 
 
 
Figure 6.8. Potential replacement for PHA of the current plastics 
 
2. Existing alternatives  
The sustainable sourcing of feedstock is a prerequisite for more sustainable products. This is the main objective of PHA production 
in this project, since normally typical PHA comes from food competing source such as the use of corn fields for the production of 
starch as raw material for polymers. Negative impacts caused by bad agricultural practice must be avoided, mainly when apply 
social criteria and human rights. T he environmental impact of plastic production should be also avoided; therefore the other 
alternative to produce PHA coming from fossil fuels is not a good choice.  
Regarding prices the PHA costs from residual streams can be reduced in order to make the process more competitive with these 
not desired alternatives, mainly respect to the plastic produced by crude oil with a market price around 1.1 kg crude oil kf-1 polymer. 
 
3. Price per unit  
Depending on which bacterial producer is used to generate PHA, the cost of production can range from $4-$16 per kg. 
However, the price should be $3-$5 per kg to be commercially viable. 
The objective in this project is: 
- 8-9 € kg-1 for mcl-PHA 
- 4-5 € kg-1 for scl-PHA 
Since the future market for mcl-PHA allow higher prices. 
 
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plan  
The bioplastic produced from module 2, coming from VFA of the first stage of AD will represent around 0.5 ton per year. 
For module 3, bioplastic coming from biomethane, the product obtained will be 0.5 ton per year. 
 
5. Market segment  
The actual market segments of PHA consist in flexible packagings and mulch films (used in blends), and some niche markets in 
consumer goods (cosmetic packagings, horticulture pots, biodegradable cutlery, etc.). The most representative PHAs are 
polyhydroxybutyrate (PHB) and poly -hydroxy valerate (PHV) and its copolymer (PHBV), with physical properties similar to 
polyethylene and polypropylene. 
In both markets, besides its physical properties, PHA provides biodegradability. This is particularly adapted to these market s 
where the product shelf -life is low. In particular in  the case of mulch films, a major advantage arises in this case in addition to 
having the advantages of a traditional plastic film: after use there is no need of collecting the film as it will degrade in soil. 
 
 
 
6. Market size

cost reductions.

The key players in the PHA market are Biomatera, Biomer, Bio -On SROL, Kaneka corporation, Meredian Ins., Metabolix Inc,

8. Market trends  
Rising demand of biodegradable materials, positive attitude of governments towards green procurement policies, and rising oil  
prices are attracting companies to invest in PHA (Polyhydroxyalkanoate) market. Many large companies such as Meredian (U.S.), 
Ecomann (China), and Bio-on (Italy) are focusing on increasing production capacities. Europe is expected to be the one of the 
largest PHA market over the next six years. Gaining importance of  biodegradable plastics coupled with regulatory support is 
expected to favor market growth in Europe. Asia Pacific would emerge as a promising market in the near future. Low production 
cost as compare to North America and Europe coupled with application growth in agriculture, food & beverage and cosmetics in 
China and India is expected to have a positive impact on PHA market in the near future. 
 
9. Barriers and risks  
The main barrier is the price difference betwee n fossil and bio-based polymers, and competition with other bio-based polymers 
such as starch-based blends and PBAT in flexible packaging and agriculture/horticulture, and PLA in consumer goods. PHA here 
produced will have however some significant advantages against other biobased polymers: a non-food competing and sustainable 
resource, and an increased biodegradability. 
 
10. Business approach 
PHA will be produced by VFAs coming from the partial AD of OFMSW, as well as from the biogas coming from the second step 
of AD process. In this case the business model is based on the technology licensing for both modules 2 and 3 with the IP rights 
belonging to URBASER, IRIAF, WR, AINIA and Nature Plast. 
 
 
 
 
COMPOSITE BIOMATERIALS 
 
1. Value proposition. 
Composite materials as used f.i in the construction, automotive and chemical industries, combine attributes from two of more 
different materials in one combined application. Examples are the combination of (bio -)plastics with natural materials/fibers 
coming from wood, wheat or starch. This, generally, leads to materials that combine the attributes of plastics (strength, low price, 
high T-tolerance) and the appearance, feel and smell of natural materials. A good example is WPC (Wood Plastic Composites) 
which has a current market value of 4,000 M€ per year and combines wood fibre with PE, PP or PVC. Within the 100% biobased 
solutions, combination of PLA, PHA, cellulose fibres and algae biomass are examples of composites e.g. used in 3D-color print 
applications. By replacing PE and other plastics by biobased alternatives as offered in this project, new 100% biobased solutions 
will also be offered for the bulk composite applications.   
 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 117 
 
 
2. Existing alternatives 
The current plastics used in composite materials – PE, PVC, PP, PBS – are all fossil-fuel based. They are generally (much) lower 
in price than the biobased alternatives. 
 
3. Price per unit 
The price would depend completely on the type of materials used for the composite and its application (1 0 € kg-1). E.g. WPC is 
cheaper than 100% wood materials. 
 
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
Within the UrBioFin-project, we will be producing sufficient composite materials based on PE, PHA, cellulose and lignin to develop 
various biobased packaging solutions for cosmetics and also various plastic bag-prototypes with different flexibilities. 
 
5. Market segment 
PE is widely used in the automotive industry, cosmetics manufactory, packaging (plastic bags, films, containers), toys, pe rsonal 
hygiene and cleaning products. The composite materials based on PE and natural fibres (cellulose, wood fibre/lignin, etc) are  
forming a rapidly growing market with a market value for WPC alone of 4,000 M € per year. 
 
6. Market size 
The total packaging market in Europe is approximately 130 billion €. Over 50% of this market is biobased (paper, board, wood) 
and 31% is plastic. The major plastic packaging material is PE -based (56%) for mostly films, wraps, and bags but also some 
bottles and containers (HD-PE) and smaller amounts of PP (19%), PET (11%), PS (10%) and PVC (4%) are used.  
In 2012, in the Netherlands, only 1 ktonne of bioplastic (0.036%) was used.  
For packaging, very few composite materials are currently available. However the possibilities are  extremely high since 
composites potentially can combine the favourable attributes of various compounds, e.g. the light weight and strength of plastic 
with the abundant availability and flexibility of paper and board. 
 
7. Growth rate 
The growth rate of biobased packaging materials is expected to be very high in the next 10 years, with EU -legislation restricting 
the use of fossil-based plastic bags in Supermarkets, etc.    
 
8. Market trends 
Bio-based PE has so far been successfully produced only in Brazil, due to the presence of large quantities of low cost feedstock 
(with Brazil the leading producer of sugar cane ethanol globally). Cheaper production of second generation bioethanol from 
alternative sources (lignocellulosic ethanol from industrial, agricultural and urban wastes) is expected to expand the PE production 
market to different regions.  
 
9. Barriers and risks 
The main barrier to the bio-based PE market expanding is the price difference between fossil and bio-based. This is partially due 
to externalisation of environmental costs for fossil products, and internalising these costs (for example via green VAT or a carbon 
tax) could reduce the price difference with fossil PE and make bio-based PE a more competitive product. Further incentive, in the 
form of volatile crude oil prices together with limited supply, had opened a market gap for bio-based chemicals at large including 
bio-based PE. In addition to cost competitiveness, there are also uncertainties which may hamper bio -based PE deplo yment. 
These include feedstock availability concerns (where raw materials such as sugarcane are also widely used in other industries), 
and competition with other competing bio-based packaging polymers such as PET, PLA and PEF87. 
 
10. Business approach 
Bio-PE will be produced by polymerisation of bioethylene coming from dehydration of second generation bioethanol from OFMSW. 
The s cl-PHA will be produced using existing mixed culture fermentation and fed with the VFA -fraction from the anaerobic 
fermentor and mcl-PHA will be produced by fermentation of Pseudomonas putida using MCFA deriving from the elongation 
fermentor as feed. Composite materials will be developed/manufactured by the Biomaterial research group of WR. Bio-PE, mcl-
PHA and s cl-PHA and various  natural materials present in the MSW (cellulose, lignin) will be blended/extruded in various 
applications. 
 
87 http://www.grandviewresearch.com/industry-analysis/bio-based-polyethylene-pe-market 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 118 
 
 
BIOMETHANE 
 
1. Value proposition. 
Natural gas (NG) plays nowadays an important role in energy provision in the EU countries to meet the demand for heat, energy 
and transport fuels. NG is a fossil based fuel which significantly contributes to greenhouse gas emissions and to the depletion of 
finite resources. In this sense, biomethane is an attractive renewable alternative that can be used as a substitute for transport 
fuels or energy production, or even as feedstock to obtain chemicals and added value products. Biomethane can be obtained 
from the upgrading of biogas. In this process, other gases contained in the biogas apart from CH 4 (CO2, H2S, VOCs, siloxanes, 
etc.) are removed in order to meet the required standards for its injection into natural gas grid.  
Despite the advantages and future prospects of biomethane, key hotspots to guarantee the sustainability of the process have 
been identified in the biomethane value chain. Among them, the feedstock for biogas production and provision and the conversion 
process of biogas to biomethane (which still represents ~25-30% of the total biomethane costs) will be addressed in URBIOFIN 
project.   
 
2. Existing alternatives 
EU energy consumption is nowadays mainly covered with fossil based fuels. For instance, from the 1683 Mtoe consumed in 2012 
in inland countries, crude oil and petrol products represented 33.8% and the share of natural gas was 23.4%. The other two main 
contributors to the total consumption were solid fuels (17.5%) and nuclear energy (13.5%)88. 
 
3. Price per unit 
Natural gas is currently sold at 0.3-0.4 € m-3, approximately 50% lower than the actual price of biomethane (~0.6 € m-3). The main 
contributors to biomethane price (including operational and capital costs of the supply chain) are the cost of raw biogas production 
and the upgrading costs, with a lower share of the distribution and refuelling stations1. 
 
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
In the prototype photobioreactor, an average algal biomass productivity of 11 -13 g m -2d-1 is expected. Based on previous 
experimental results from the UVA research group, 12 Nm3 of biogas will be daily upgraded to biomethane.  
 
5. Market segment 
The potential uses of biomethane are similar to those of natural gas: production of power and heat, direct use as automotive fuel, 
and feedstock for the production of many different products (paints, plastics, detergents, etc.). 
 
6. Market size 
About 3.8 × 10 11 m3 of natural gas were used in 2014 for primary energy consumption. In contrast, current production of 
biomethane accounts for 1.4 Bn m 3, of which only 10% is used in transport. Therefore , there is a potential market available for 
biomethane as a substitute of natural gas. 
 
7. Growth rate 
According to the European Biogas Association, in 2014 there were 367 biomethane plants in Europe, of which 70% injected to 
natural gas grids. These plants e xhibit a biomethane upgrading capacity of 200,000 Nm 3 h-1, producing yearly ~ 1.4 Bn m 3 of 
biomethane.This biomethane upgraded from biogas represents a very low percentage of the total biogas production (only 2% of 
the total biogas was upgraded in 2010), however recent estimations from the European Biogas Association revealed that about 
37.5 % of the total production of biogas will be upgraded to biomethane in 2030 (18 Bn m 3) and used for grid injection and fuel. 
The advantages of this product in terms of e nergy sustainability and reduction of GHG emissions will certainly contribute to the 
growth of the European biomethane market.  
 
8. Market trends 
So far, biogas upgrading is mainly undertaken in Europe, with minor upgrading activities in the USA or South Kore a89. The 
biomethane production market is expected to expand promoted by a cheaper conversion of the biogas obtained from OMSW to 
biomethane. This expansion will be especially significant in those countries with a good natural gas infrastructure, where th e 
integration of biomethane into the existing energy system will be relatively easy. 
 
88 http://ec.europa.eu/energy/sites/ener/files/documents/2014_energy_market_en.pdf 
89 http://www.bioenergytrade.org/downloads/t40-t37-biomethane-2014.pdf 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 119 
 
 
 
9. Barriers and risks 
Potential limiting factors and risks associated to biomethane include different sections of the biomethane value chain. One of the 
main concerns is the use of feedstock for energetic purposes, and many biogas plants are located on farms with little economic 
advantages for biogas upgrading. URBIOFIN propose the use of OMSW for biogas production and subsequent upgrading, thus 
ensuring the sustainability of the feedstock. Moreover, the upgrading system, based on a biological photosynthetic process, will 
provide additional treatment of the digestate thus reducing the environmental impacts of this waste. The low economic efficiency 
of the biomethane mainly assoc iated to biogas production and upgrading costs also hinder the actual competitiveness of 
biomethane as a substitute of natural gas. The approach here proposed will significantly reduce the costs entailed by the cleaning 
process by substituting current physical-chemical upgrading technologies with cost-efficient biological methods.  
 
10. Business approach 
Biogas will be first produced by anaerobic digestion of OMSW, and further upgraded to biomethane by a photosynthetic based 
technology to reach the required quality for its use as automotive fuel. The model business is based on the technology licensing 
for the upgrading of biogas to a biomethane that can be used as a non -fossil fuel substituting natural gas.  Intellectual property 
rights on the associa ted technologies belong to Universidad de Valladolid and Urbaser, the terms defined in the Consortium 
agreement. 
 
 
SOLID AND LIQUID BIOFERTILISERS 
 
1. Value proposition. 
The URBIOFIN is based on the recovery of waste materials, (OFMSW in this case with an estimated annual generation in the EU 
of  100,000,000 t per year), using the downstream intermediate and final products from bioEthanol annd bioMethane producion. 
Stabilised organic matter solid fraction from End-of-Waste separate collection, can be an excellent raw material for elaboration of 
substrates, organic fertilisers and biofertilisers, with the requirements of no impurities and pollutants present, low conductivity, no 
re-fermentation and temperature rise episodes.  
In this case, thanks to the expertise of different partners (NOVO, AINIA, BPE, WR, others) it is proposed the development of a 
line of solid biofertilisers that will be produced, characterised, tested in laboratory, greenhouse and field and throughly evaluated. 
These segment of solid biofertilisers is more competitive with traditional mineral and organo -mineral fertilisers, with greater size  
but lower value added. 
Inoculants, as selected bacteria strains will be sup plied inside URBIOFIN partners or thans to the cooperation and commercial 
links established by the Task leader, BPE, with different companies and research centres.    
Regarding liquid biofertilisers, microalgae grown on AD liquid and gaseous streams will be used as protein source for aminoacids 
production via hydrolysis, to be used as main component of liquid biofertilisers. Additives and or inoculants could also be added 
for specific formulations and designed products. Liquid biofertilisers in general and based in aminoacid and peptids in particular, 
are a fast growing but smaller market but with much higher value added products, designated typically for fertigation and fruit and 
vegetables intensive crops. 
 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 120 
 
 
 
Figure 6.11. Biofertilisers global market share 
 
2. Existing alternatives 
In the biofetilisers market there is already a growing group of companies offering their products to the market. The market is very 
disperse and together with the lack of regulation, the situation has been favourable to the rapid spreading of small and innovative 
companies offering their products, normally diverse from case to case and difficult to compare. 
In the specific case of liquid fertilisers composed of aminoacids and/or polypeptides instead the market has been developing for 
the last 40 years, and there are many medium size and big companies offering this type of product, some of them departing from 
macroalgae and sea weeds, many of them based in Asia. Some of the international market leading companies are the following1: 
- Gujarat State Fertilizers & Chemicals Ltd. (India),  
- Novozymes A/S (Denmark),   
- Rizobacter Argentina S.A. (Argentina),  
- Camson Bio Technologies Limited (India),  
- Lallemand Inc. (Canada),  
- National Fertilizers Limited (India),  
- Madras Fertilizers Limited (India),  
- T. Stanes & Company Limited (India),  
- Rashtriya Chemicals & Fertilizers Ltd. (India), and  
- Nutramax Laboratories Inc. (U.S.) 
 
3. Price per unit 
Prices for the s olid and liquid formulation will depend on its formulation and evaluation of the ir agricultural effects, yet to be 
demonstrated in this case. 
For previous calculations the prices that have been assigned to the new bioproducts are in line with similar products already  
present in the market. 
Solid dry granules biofertiliser price has been estimated in 0.35 € t-1. 
Liquid aminoacids biofertiliser price has been estimated in 6 € L-1 or kg-1. 
  
4. Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plan. 
Production of solid and liquid biofertilisers will be a sideline  of the “OFMSW to Bioethanol” demo plant. Production of these 
products will be performed at “Algodor R&D&I and Experimental Production Centre” in Toledo Spain. The capacity of production 
in this Centre is about 2,500 t onnes per year of solid biofertilisers. Capacity for production of Liquid biofertilisers is about 1,000 
tonnes per year, but could easily be increased in ca se of necessity, although the limiting factor for the capacity production in 
URBIOFIN project will be the production of microalgae in the photobioreactor for biomethane upgrading set up at URBASER 
research and development centre in Zaragoza, so a bigger installation for microalgae production at low cost should be 
implemented in this case. 
5. Market segment 

                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 121 
 
 
Biofertilizers also known as “plant pr obiotics”, by its favourable effects through the interaction between microorganisms, plants 
and the environment  represent a segment of agricultural inputs (seeds, agri-chemicals, pesticides, etc), with a great potential of 
substitution of the conventional  agrichemicals. They have particular characteristics regarding the absence of involvement of 
chemical and/or synthetic products in its fabrication and consequently those products are suitable for its use in the organic or 
biological farming. 
Due to their novelty and rapid growing, Biofertilisers and Biostimulants are lacking of a regulatory frame in most Member Sta tes 
and in the EU, although it is foreseen its regulation in the revision of Directive 2003/2003 CE on Fertilisers.  
There are diff erent associations at Member State, European or international level that integrate the companies participating in 
this emergent segment of the production of agricultural inputs: 
- AEFA - Asociación Española de Fabricantes de Agro-nutrientes http://aefa-agronutrientes.org/ 
- EBIC - European Biostimulants Industry Consortium http://www.biostimulants.eu/ 
- IBMA – International Biocontrol Manufacturers Association www.ibma-global.org/ 
Regarding its application field or method categories of biofertilisers could be stated as follows: 
Types by effect: 
• Nitrogen-fixing 
• Phosphate-solubilising 
• Potash-mobilizing 
• Others (zinc, boron, and sulfur-solubilising biofertilisers) 
By crops: 
• Cereals & grains 
• Pulses & oilseeds 
• Fruits & vegetables 
• Others (turf & ornamentals, plantation crops, fiber crops, spices, and silage & forage crops) 
By microorganisms components: 
• Rhizobium 
• Azotobacter 
• Azospirillum 
• Cyanobacteria 
• Phosphate solubilising bacteria 
• Others (potash-mobilizing, zinc biofertilisers, sulfur-solubilising biofertilisers, and manganese solubiliser) 
By application types: 
• Seed 
• Soil 
• Others (set treatment, foliar treatment, root dipping, and seedling root treatment) 
 
6. Market size, growth rate and market trend 
The increasing awareness regarding health and wellness, the growing organic food industry, and the significant increase in th e 
price of chemicals have played a huge role in providing the necessary boost to the global biofertilisers industry. Global 
Biofertilisers Market is expected to reach USD 1.88 Billion by 2020 at a CAGR of 14.0% from 2015 to 2020. The phosphate -
solubilising segment is the fastest- growing market in the biofertilisers industry across the globe. 
 
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                                
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 122 
 
 
 
Figure 6.12. Biofertilisers global market size by types 
 
The market was dominated by North America in 2014 and accounted for the largest share in the total biofertilisers market. Asia-
Pacific is projected to be the fastest-growing market for between 2015 and 2020 due to the increasing prices of chemical fertilizers 
and increasing food demand due to population growth. In terms of type, the nitrogen fixing biofertilisers segment is projected to 
be the largest market for biofertilisers; whereas in terms of crop -type, cereals & grains is projected to be the largest market for 
biofertilisers in 2014. The cyanobacteria segment is projected to be the largest market for biofertilisers by 2020 in terms of 
microorganisms. Furthermore, by application, seed treatment is projected to be the largest market for biofertilisers in between 
2015 and 2020. 
The biofertilisers market is characterized by intense competition due to the presence of a large number of both large and small-
scale firms. Expansions, acquisitions, agreements and new product launches are the key strategies adopted by market players 
to ensure their growth in the market. 
 
7. Barriers and risks 
The main barriers and risks for the development of this value proposition are the following: 
- The risk for competition and price/efficiency barrier with other products already present in the market, with whom newly 
designed and produced biofertilisers have to be compared in tests and field trials.   
- The foreseen revision of Directive 2003/2003 CE o n Fertilisers, that will regulate Biofertilisers and Biostimulants, not 
regulated yet. 
- The future costs of product development and registration at EU and international level. 
- The challenge of producing relatively high amounts of microalgae at a low cost, f or liquid aminoacids biofertiliser 
production. 
- The risk of effectively and safely perform the field trials in order to obtain a reliable evaluation of the newly designed and 
produced biofertilisers, and the need of repetitive consistent good results at these evaluation tests. 
 
8. Business approach 
The solid and liquid biofertilisers valorisation line at URBIOFIN is a sideline of the main “demo” Plant for OFMSW conversion in 
bioethanol and the use of bioethanol as building block for other added value chemicals (bioplastics, bioethylene, etc). 
Using as raw materials the potential enormous amounts of stabilised liquid and solid biowaste coming from the fermentation / AD 
processes, is open a new window for the valorisation of these residuals and sidestreams of biowaste management via the 
Biorefinery concept, in which the connection between waste management companies (URBASER, BPE),  technology suppliers 
(IMECAL, CIEMAT), biotechnology companies (NOVOZYMES), RTOs (CIEMAT, AINIA, WR) and industries and SMEs 
companies, should be adjusted and still work beyond the project duration. 
The selected and separated origin of the biowaste and th e complex and careful selection and stabilisation process should 
guarantee the absence of impurities and pollutants, that would open the possibility of registration of the final products by End-of-
Waste label (out of the waste management scope regulations). 
Stable alliances between involved partners with the inclusion of specific inoculants or production technology suppliers and RTOs 
could lead to develop a long term successful business strategy for the production and commercialisation of biofertilisers.
